Kinetics of enzyme-enhanced CO2 capture 
 
Submitted by 
 
Randi Neerup s100236 
 
Supervisors: 
 
Associate Professor Nicolas von Solms 
 
Ph. D student Arne Gladis 
 
 
24-01-2016      
 
Department of Chemical and Biochemical Engineering 
 
Technical University of Denmark 
==PAGE:2==
I 
 
 
 
==PAGE:3==
II 
 
Abstract  
The CO2 absorption performance in methyldiethanolamine (MDEA) solvents containing the enzyme, 
carbonic anhydrase (CA) was studied in a wetted wall column setup. The solvent was tested at three 
different concentrations from 15 to 50 wt% in aqueous solution and with CA concentrations from 0 to 
0.3 wt% at 298, 313 and 328 K and with CO2 loading ranging from 0 to 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent.  
In order to determine mass transfer parameters such as the liquid side mass transfer coefficient, physical 
material properties as density and viscosity were measured in the lab and based on these results 
correlations were proposed. The average prediction was ± 0.5 % and ± 3 % for density and viscosity 
respectively.  
The equilibrium partial pressure of CO2 in the solvents from the experiments were compared to the 
Extended UNIQUAC model. There were a good agreement between the model prediction and the 
measured data for 15 and 30 wt% MDEA with an enzyme concentration of 0.15 and 0.3 wt%. The 
enzyme did not change the thermodynamic properties of the MDEA solvents. 
Addition of CA, enhanced the liquid side mass transfer coefficient by a factor up to 9 times in MDEA. 
The study revealed that the highest mass transfer occurs at 298 K and at low MDEA concentration (15 
wt%). Increasing CO2 concentrations resulted in a considerably decrease in the liquid side mass transfer. 
The overall kinetic constant of CA (1/s) was linear dependent on the enzyme concentration, which meant 
that first order kinetics could be applied. The derived enzymatic rate constant (m3/(mol·s)) was 
decreasing with increasing temperatures and increasing solvent concentrations. Adding CO2 to the 
solvents resulted in a considerably decrease of the enzymatic rate constant. By proposing a simple 
model, which accounted for bicarbonate inhibition, it was possible to predict the catalytic activity of CA 
with respect to CO2 loading within an average of ± 14 %.  
15 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA showed the best absorption performance under all investigated 
process conditions as it was 34 % faster than 30 wt% MDEA and 60 % faster than 50 wt% MDEA with 
the same amount of enzyme at 298 and 313 K. At 328 K, it was 16 % and 40 % faster than in 30 wt% 
and 50 wt% MDEA solutions respectively. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:4==
III 
 
 
 
==PAGE:5==
IV 
 
Resumé 
Absorptionshastigheden af CO2 i vandige opløsninger af methyldiethanolamin (MDEA) indeholdende 
enzymet kulsyreanhydrase (CA) blev undersøgt i en befugtet vægkolonne (wetted wall column). 
Hastigheden hvormed CO2 absorberes i MDEA opløsningerne blev testet ved temperaturene 298, 313 
og 328 K og med et CO2 indhold fra 0 til 0.5 mol CO2/mol opløsning. Absorption blev målt i vandige 
opløsninger med MDEA koncentrationer på henholdsvis 15, 30 og 50 vægtprocent og med et 
enzymindhold fra 0 til 0.3 vægtprocent.  
Massefylde og viskositet blev målt for at kunne bestemme vigtige massetransportparametre såsom 
masseoverføringskoefficienten på væskesiden. Baseret på disse resultater blev der udviklet 
korrelationer, som gør det muligt at interpolere mellem de målte områder. Den gennemsnitlige afvigelse 
var ± 0.5 % og ± 3 % for henholdsvis massefylde og viskositet.  
Partialtrykket af CO2 i væsken blev sammenlignet med den udvidede UNIQUAC model og det viste sig, 
der var en god overensstemmelse mellem model og de målte resultater for opløsninger med en MDEA 
koncentration på 15 og 30 vægtprocent og med enzymkoncentrationer på 0.15 og 0.3 vægtprocent. 
Enzymet ændrede ikke de termodynamiske egenskaber af MDEA opløsningerne. 
Tilsætning af CA forbedrede masseoverføringskoefficienten på væskesiden med en faktor optil 9 i 
MDEA opløsningerne. Undersøgelsen viste, at den største masseoverføring skete ved 298 K og ved lave 
MDEA koncentrationer (15 wt%). Høje CO2 koncentrationer resulterede i et betydeligt fald i 
massetransporten. Den overordnet kinetiske konstant af CA (1/s) var lineært afhængig af 
enzymkoncentrationen, hvilket betyder at første ordens reaktionskinetik kan anvendes. Den afledte 
enzymatiske hastighedskonstant (m3/(mol·s)) var faldende ved stigende temperaturer og MDEA 
koncentrationer. Tilsætning af CO2 til opløsningerne resulterede i et betydeligt fald i 
hastighedskonstanten af CA. Ved at foreslå en simpel model, som kunne forudsige inhiberingen af 
bikarbonat, var det muligt at bestemme den katalytiske aktivitet af CA med hensyn til CO2 indhold, med 
en gennemsnitlig afvigelse på ± 14 %.  
15 vægtprocent MDEA med en enzymkoncentration på 0.15 vægtprocent viste sig at have den bedste 
absorption ydeevne, da den ved 298 og 313 K var 34 % hurtigere end i 30 vægtprocent MDEA og 60 % 
hurtigere end i 50 vægtprocent MDEA. Ved 328 K, var absorption ydeevnen henholdsvis 16 % og 40 % 
hurtigere end i 30 og 50 vægtprocent MDEA opløsninger.

VI 
 
Preface 
This thesis is the final part of the M.Sc. in Chemical and Biochemical Engineering at the Technical 
University of Denmark (DTU). The work has been carried out during the period August 2015 to January 
2016 at the Center for Energy Resources Engineering (CERE), Department of Chemical and 
Biochemical Engineering. The M.Sc. thesis accounts for 30 ETCS.  
I would like to thank my two supervisors Associate Professor Nicolas von Solms and Ph. D. student 
Arne Gladis for the support and guidance throughout this project. Especially, Arne Gladis for the 
opportunity to work on a small part of his exciting Ph.D. project and for the advice and discussions in 
the experimental work.    
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   24-01-2016 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Randi Neerup

==PAGE:24==
Chapter 1 - Introduction 
 
 
Page 1 of 134 
 
1   Introduction 
Carbon capture technology provides the possibility to reduce carbon dioxide (CO2) emissions by removing 
CO2 from exhaust gases such as power plant flue gas. In typical solvent-based capture processes, CO2 is 
captured from a gaseous mixture by a chemical solvent in an absorber column and pure CO2 is released under 
input of thermal energy in the desorber column. One approach to make this process economically more feasible 
is the use of kinetic promoters in otherwise slow-absorbing solvents. Slow-absorbing solvents such as tertiary 
alkanolamines are considered energetically more favourable in the desorption process due to the low heats of 
reaction with CO2. The enzyme carbonic anhydrase (CA) has attracted interest in recent years as the enzyme 
has shown the possibility to increase the CO2 absorption rate for slow solvents several fold. It is particularly 
attractive when the solvents have a high capacity for CO2 whereas the rate of absorption is low. 
The objective of this project is to find correlations describing the effect of adding enzyme to the absorption 
performance of the solvent. Absorption/desorption experiments were carried out in a wetted wall column 
(WWC) reactor with the tertiary amine solvent methyldiethanolamine (MDEA) at varying process conditions 
such as enzyme concentration, solvent concentration, CO2 concentration and temperature.  
In order to interpret the experimental data and derive kinetic constants several physicochemical material 
properties such as density and viscosity are needed. The effect of carbonic anhydrase on these properties was 
measured in the lab and correlations were developed allowing interpolation between the measured ranges. 
Kinetic constants were derived from the experimental results and a simple kinetic model is proposed describing 
the effect of CO2 loading on the CA enhanced absorption rate.   
 
 
 
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2 - Background 
 
 
Page 2 of 134 
 
2   Background  
2.1 Emission of Greenhouse Gases  
The world’s energy demand is increasing steadily due to economic growth and development in particular from 
the developing countries. Most of the world’s energy has up to now been generated by combustion of fossil 
fuel, which has led to a significant increase in the emission of greenhouse gases (GHG) and especially of CO2. 
The high amount of CO2 released into the atmosphere causes the earth’s temperature to increase. According to 
the International Energy Agency (IEA) [1] the concentration of CO2 has been rising since the beginning of the 
industrial revolution in the 1800 century. The atmospheric level of CO2 measured by the National Oceanic and 
Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) research laboratory in Mauna Loa, Hawaii reached 398.84 ppm in 
August 2015 [2]. This is an increase of 40 % compared to the level before the industrial revolution [1]. 
The increased emission of CO2 is considered by the majority of expert scientific opinion to be mainly caused 
by anthropogenic activities. Figure 1 represents the shares of the global human-made emissions of greenhouse 
gases. As illustrated in the figure the global energy sector accounted for 69 % of the emissions in 2010.   
 
Figure 1: Estimates of the global shares of man-made GHG emissions in 2010  [1]. 
The majority of the world’s energy is produced from fossil fuels such as oil and coal. Despite a growing interest 
in using other alternatives such as wind and biomass energy the use of fossil sources accounted for 82 % of 
the energy generation globally in 2012 [1]. The World Energy Outlook [3] predicts that the global energy  and 
coal demand will grow by 37 and 15 % to 2040 respectively. According to IEA Greenhouse gas R & D 
programme [4] coal fired power plants are less efficient and generate more CO2 compared to natural gas fired 
plants. An overview of the efficiency and CO2 emission from coal-, petroleum- and gas-fired power plants are 
listed in Table 1. The table indicates that coal-fired power stations are the major source of emission of CO2 
contributing approximately 750 g/kWh.  
Industrial processes 6 %
Agriculture 11 %
Others 14 %
CH4 9 %
N2O 1 %
Energy 69 %
CO2 90 %
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2 - Background 
 
 
Page 3 of 134 
 
Table 1: Energy efficiency (%) and carbon dioxide emission (g/kWh) for coal-, petroleum- and gas-fired power plants [4].  
Plant

Natural Gas

~ 350 
In order to mitigate the growing concern of anthropogenic CO2 emission, the carbon capture technology is a 
promising approach, which provides the possibility of nearly immediate reduction of emissions from fossil 
fuel burning- and natural gas power plants, by retrofitting to existing plants   
2.2 Carbon Capture Technology  
Carbon capture from coal-fired power plants is generally divided into three approaches: pre-combustion, oxy-
fuel combustion and post-combustion. After the CO2 is captured, it can be stored underground, or used for 
enhanced oil recovery and other purposes.  
The following section introduces the three capturing techniques including advantages and drawbacks of the 
different approaches. The injection and storage of CO2 are a very complex topic that is outside the scope of 
this thesis.  
2.2.1 Pre-combustion 
In pre-combustion capture, carbon dioxide from process streams is captured before the fuel is combusted and 
an example of the process is depicted in Figure 2. Coal is gasified with oxygen in an integrated gasification 
combined cycle (IGCC) into syngas [5] represented by (A) in the figure. The syngas contains CO and H2. The 
syngas is converted to CO2 and H2 in a water-gas shift reactor (B), which increases the CO2 concentration. 
Impurities such as sulphur are removed from the stream before CO2 is separated from H2 and compressed 
represented by (C) and (D) respectively. CO2 is removed using chemical or physical solvents. A physical 
solvent could be Selexol or Rectisol [5][6]. H2 from the capture process is used as fuel to generate power (E).  
 
Figure 2: Illustration of pre-combustion CO2 capture [6]. A: Gasifier, B: Water-gas shift reactor, C: Sulphur removal, D: CO2 capture 
unit, E: Power generation, F: Air separation unit.  
A 
B 
C 
D 
E 
F 
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2 - Background 
 
 
Page 4 of 134 
 
2.2.2 Oxy-fuel combustion  
In oxy-fuel combustion the fossil fuel is combusted (A) with oxygen (purity greater than 95%) [5] depicted in 
Figure 3. Combustion with pure oxygen reduces the formation of NOx and other impurities significantly and 
results in a stream mainly containing CO2. The gas stream is cleaned using lime (B) and pure CO2 is 
compressed (C). Combustion with pure oxygen and coal is a high temperature process, which most materials 
cannot withstand therefore the exhaust gas is recycled to the combustion unit in order to maintain a reasonable 
temperature [5]. The heat generated from the combustion of oxygen and fossil fuel (A) is used to run a steam 
turbine (E).  
 
Figure 3: Illustration of oxy-fuel combustion CO2 capture [6]. A: Boiler, B: Flue gas clean-up, C: CO2 compression unit, D: Air 
separation unit, E: Steam turbine.  
2.2.3 Post-combustion  
In post-combustion capture, the CO2 is captured from the flue gas after combustion. This process is illustrated 
in Figure 4. Fossil fuel are mixed with atmospheric air and combusted in a boiler/superheater (A). Heat 
generated from the combustion is used for the steam turbine (B) in order to produce electric power and steam 
for the CO2 capture process. Impurities in the flue gas are removed after combustion with a scrubber (C) in 
order to prevent unwanted chemical reactions. Afterwards CO2 can be separated from the flue gas (D) and 
compressed (E) for further transportation and storage. A more detailed description of post-combustion capture 
is described in chapter 3. 
A 
B
C
D
E
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2 - Background 
 
 
Page 5 of 134 
 
 
Figure 4: Illustration of post-combustion CO2 capture [6]. A: Boiler, B: Steam turbine, C: Sulphur removal, D: CO2 capture unit, E: 
CO2 compression.  
2.2.4 Overview of Advantages and Drawbacks 
Each of the three approaches for CO2 capture has its advantages and disadvantages, which are summarised in 
Table 2. Post-combustion capture can easily be retrofitted to already existing power plants as this technology 
applies to e.g. natural gas and coal-fired plants. Whereas pre-combustion capture applies to gasification plants 
and oxy-fuel combustion applies mostly to new plants [5].  
Table 2: Advantages and disadvantages for the three CO2 capture approaches [5][6].  
 
Advantages 
Disadvantages 
Pre-combustion 
 
Improved CO2 capture 
efficiency  
 
Only applicable to new plants 
 
Barriers to commercial use 
 
Required cooling of syngas 
Oxy-fuel combustion 
 
Lower emissions of NOx 
 
No capture facility 
 
High cost of air separation 
Post-combustion 
 
Retrofitted to existing plant 
 
Applies to coal fired power 
plants 
 
Low conc. of CO2 at atmospheric 
pressure (approx. 15 %) 
 
High energy penalty 
 
Solvent loss 
 
One of the advantages of pre-combustion is the capture of CO2 under high pressure. This leads to a higher 
driving force for the CO2 capture and results in a reduced energy penalty (approx. 20%) compared to post-
combustion capture and reduced equipment costs [5]. The use of IGCC in pre-combustion processes makes it 
suitable for solid and liquid fuels such as coal, petroleum and biomass [7]. The drawback of pre-combustion 
capture is the absence of commercial demonstrations as the capital costs of implementing IGCC are high [5]. 
The Kemper County pre-combustion IGCC plant was planned to start in May 2015, but the project has so far 
been delayed due to cost-increases [8]. Another drawback is the need of cooling the syngas because of physical 
solvents have the best CO2 capacity at moderate temperature.  
The absence of a CO2 capture unit is an advantage for the oxy-fuel combustion technology that significantly 
reduces the equipment costs. The use of pure oxygen in the combustion ensures that no NOx is present in the 
gas stream and a clean-up unit can be skipped [6]. Oxy-fuel combustion is limited to new plants, as the whole 
A 
B
C
D
E
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2 - Background 
 
 
Page 6 of 134 
 
power plant needs to be implemented with this technology. This also means that the oxy-fuel combustion 
approach has not yet been tried on pilot scale level [9]. The use of pure O2 in the technology is expensive as 
O2 is separated from air using cryogenic air separation [10]. 
The major advantage of the post-combustion technology is that it can readily be retrofitted to existing power 
plants. The technology is well established as a lot of research has been conducted in the field of gas purification 
using liquid solvents [11]. Furthermore, the technology has been demonstrated on a pilot scale basis [12]–[16], 
which is an advantage when considering commercialization. A challenge for the post-combustion process is 
that existing plants use air for combustion, generating a flue gas that has a diluted concentration of CO2 of 12-
13 vol% [17] at atmospheric pressure leading to a low driving force of CO2 [5]. Amine solutions are used as 
chemical absorbents for the capture of CO2. The current amine technology needs to be improved, as the loss 
of solvent and a high-energy penalty are associated with the regeneration process making the technology 
expensive.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 3 – Solvent Technology for Post-Combustion CO2 Capture 
 
 
Page 7 of 134 
 
3   Solvent technology for post-combustion CO2 capture 
Purification of gases using alkanolamines as absorbents has been applied since the 1930s [11]. The principle 
behind absorption of CO2 using aqueous alkanolamine solutions is the reaction of a weak base and a weak acid 
forming bicarbonate and/or carbamate.  
A simplified flowsheet of a post-combustion capture plant is outlined in Figure 5. In the CO2 capture process, 
the flue gas enters the absorber column from the bottom at 40 °C counter-current with the lean solvent that is 
fed in on the top of the column. The rich solvent, containing absorbed CO2, leaving the bottom of the absorber 
is heated in a cross heat exchanger with the lean solvent coming from the desorber column. The desorber is 
usually operated at 100-120 °C. The rich solvent enters the desorber and CO2 is released at the top. The hot 
lean solvent from the desorber is cooled in the cross heat exchanger and recycled to the absorber [11]. The 
inlet concentration of CO2 in the flue gas is between 10-15 vol% [17][18] [19] for coal combustion and it is 
acquired to capture approximately 90 vol% [5][20]. For a conventional absorption/desorption process using 
ethanolamine (MEA) as solvent, the lean solvent has a CO2 loading between 0.1-0.2 mol CO2/mol solvent and 
the rich solvent has a loading between 0.4-0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent [21]. 
 
Figure 5: Simplified flow sheet of a CO2 capture plant [11] [19]. 
In order to select a suitable solvent for the CO2 absorption process, reaction mechanisms, kinetics and mass 
transfer must be considered.   
3.1 Alkanolamine solvents 
The most common solvents used for the post-combustion CO2 capture are aqueous amine solutions. Amines 
are derivatives of ammonia and are divided into three main subgroups: primary, secondary and tertiary amines. 
Characteristic to all alkanolamines is that they have at least one amino- and one hydroxyl group. According to 
Kohl et al. [11] the amino group causes the absorption of CO2 due to the alkalinity in water and the hydroxyl 
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 3 – Solvent Technology for Post-Combustion CO2 Capture 
 
 
Page 8 of 134 
 
group serves to reduce the vapour pressure. Some of the more commonly used amines are listed in Table 3. 
This section introduces the reaction mechanisms and kinetics of primary, secondary and tertiary amines.  
Table 3: Commonly used amines for CO2 capture. I: Primary, II: Secondary, III: Tertiary.

3.1.1 Primary and Secondary Amines 
The zwitterion mechanism, originally proposed by Caplow [22] is the generally accepted mechanism of the 
reaction of carbon dioxide with primary or secondary amines. The zwitterion mechanism includes two steps 
[19]. The first step is the formation of a CO2-amine intermediate, which is represented by reaction (i). The 
second step is a base catalysed deprotonation of the zwitterion resulting in the formation of carbamate, 
represented by reaction (ii), where B is the base:

Primary and secondary amines are known to have high reaction rates. However, limitations are associated with 
the use of the amines such as a high heat of absorption, degradation and corrosion [26]. An aqueous solution 
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 3 – Solvent Technology for Post-Combustion CO2 Capture 
 
 
Page 10 of 134 
 
of the primary amine MEA of 30 wt% is used as a reference solvent for the capture process. MEA is known to 
have fast absorption kinetics as the MEA molecule has a lone pair of electrons available in the amine group, 
which makes it very reactive towards carbon dioxide [19]. Several drawbacks are related to the use of MEA 
for CO2 capture. The heat of absorption released per mole of CO2 absorbed in MEA is 72 kJ and regeneration 
of the solvent is approximated to 165 kJ per mole of desorbed CO2 [29]. Yeh et al. [29] estimated that 80 % of 
the total capture process is due to the extra heat required for desorption  making the process very expensive. 
Additionally, operational problems occur such as solvent loss, degradation products and corrosion 
[17][30][31].   
3.1.2 Tertiary Amines 
A different reaction mechanism is shown for tertiary amines that do not include the formation of an amine 
carbamate. The reaction of a tertiary amine with CO2 results in the formation of bicarbonate.  
 
𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐻2𝑂+ 𝑅1𝑅2𝑅3𝑁
𝑘2
→𝑅1𝑅2𝑅3𝐻𝑁+ + 𝐻𝐶𝑂3
−  
(v) 
The reaction rate of CO2 is expressed as a second order reaction presented in equation (2). Slow reaction kinetic 
with CO2 is associated with tertiary amines as all hydrogen atoms are replaced by organic substituents. 
However, higher loading capacity up to 1.0 mol CO2/mol of amine are observed according to literature 
[23][32]. The parallel reactions (iii) and (iv) occur simultaneously, which gives an overall reaction rate constant 
𝑘𝑜𝑣 (1/s) of [33]: 
 
𝑘𝑜𝑣= 𝑘𝑂𝐻−𝐶𝑂𝐻−+ 𝑘𝐻2𝑂𝐶𝐻2𝑂+ 𝑘𝑎𝑚𝐶𝑎𝑚 
(7) 
The kinetics of the tertiary amine MDEA with CO2 has been studied by many scientists [25][26][34]. Although 
the reaction kinetic is slow for MDEA, Carson et al. [35] measured that the heat of absorption is lower than 
MEA. This is an advantage as it results in a lower regeneration energy giving a reduced cost of the desorption 
unit [33].  
3.1.3 Blends of Amines 
Recently blends of amines in the CO2 capture process have gained more interest. Blends of amines consist of 
tertiary amines with a small amount of either primary or secondary amines also known as promoters. This 
would theoretically give a solution that combines the low regeneration energy and the high loading of the 
tertiary amine with the high absorption rate of the primary or secondary amine [33]. Versteeg et al. [24] 
proposed that the reaction rate of blended amines could be affected by either the zwitterion mechanism or the 
occurrence of several parallel reactions. 
A well-known promoter is the amine piperazine (PZ). Several researchers including Bishnoi [36], Dang et al. 
[37] and Dugas et al. [38] have concluded that PZ accelerates the absorption of CO2 in amine solutions. The 
drawbacks of chemically accelerators are that they change the solubility and are consumed during reaction 
[39]. Freeman et al. [40] concluded that the disadvantage of using PZ is the low solubility. Increasing the PZ 
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 3 – Solvent Technology for Post-Combustion CO2 Capture 
 
 
Page 11 of 134 
 
concentration may create precipitation at room temperature [41]. Furthermore, is PZ toxic to humans and 
environment.  
A relatively new approach is the addition of a biocatalyst in particular the enzyme carbonic anhydrase in 
combination with aqueous tertiary amine solutions to facilitate enhanced absorption rates. The focus of this 
thesis is the use of carbonic anhydrase in aqueous MDEA solutions and will be described in chapter 4. 
3.2 Alternative Solvents 
Alternative chemical solvents for the CO2 capture technology are also considered. Amino acids and potassium 
carbonate based solvents are reviewed in the following sections.  
3.2.1 Amino Acid Based Solvents 
Amino acids are naturally occurring and are considered potential candidates for CO2 capture. The reaction 
kinetics are comparable to primary and secondary alkanolamines and are according to Hook [42] resistant 
toward degradation and have no environmental or toxic impact.  
Aqueous amino acids can exist in a zwitterionic, acidic or basic state. The zwitterionic and acidic states are 
not reactive towards CO2 as the amino group is protonated and the carboxylic group is deprotonated [43]. 
Addition of a strong base deprotonates the zwitterionic state [44]:  
 
𝑂𝐻−+  +𝑁𝐻2𝑅1𝑅2𝐶𝑂𝑂−⇄𝐻2𝑂+ 𝑁𝐻𝑅1𝑅2𝐶𝑂𝑂− 
(vi) 
The deprotonated amino acid reacts with CO2 forming a carbamate intermediate: 
 
𝐶𝑂2 + 𝑁𝐻𝑅1𝑅2𝐶𝑂𝑂−
𝑘2
⇄
𝑘−1
  −𝑂𝑂𝐶𝑁𝐻+𝑅1𝑅2𝐶𝑂𝑂− 
(vii) 
A proton is removed from the carbamate by a base B forming a neutral carbamate: 
 
𝐵 +  −𝑂𝑂𝐶𝑁𝐻+𝑅1𝑅2𝐶𝑂𝑂−𝑘𝑏
→   −𝑂𝑂𝐶𝑁𝑅1𝑅2𝐶𝑂𝑂−+ 𝐵𝐻+ 
(viii) 
Reaction (vii) is the rate-limiting step and this reduces the rate expression to a second order similar to that of 
alkanolamines, equation (2) [44]. 
3.2.2 Potassium Carbonate Solvents 
Potassium carbonate (K2CO3) is considered an alternative solvent for the CO2 capture process due to the low 
heat of regeneration and low toxicity [44][45]. The hot carbonated process was originally developed by Benson 
et al. [46] in the 1950’s. In this process, CO2 is absorbed under pressure using hot carbonate solutions. The 
process has later been improved by addition of promoters to enhance the CO2 absorption rate [45][47]. 
Recently, amino acids and/or carbonic anhydrase promoted potassium carbonate solutions have been studied 
all showing improved rate of absorption [44][48]. 
The reaction mechanism for hot K2CO3 and CO2 is represented by the following reaction [47]: 
==PAGE:35==

Page 12 of 134 
 
 
𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐶𝑂3
2−+ 𝐻2𝑂⇆2𝐻𝐶𝑂3
− 
(ix) 
which can be described in terms of two parallel reactions (x) and (xi). In reaction (x), the carbonate ion reacts 
with water forming OH-. The hydroxyl ion reacts with carbon dioxide in reaction (xi) forming bicarbonate.

The reaction rate in equation (xi) is rate limiting, which gives a second order expression similar to the 
expression of amines (equation (2)) [47].

Chapter 4 – Carbonic Anhydrase (CA)

4   Carbonic Anhydrase (CA)  
Enzymes have been used for many years as components in a wide range of industrial applications as textile, 
food and pharmaceuticals. The use of enzymes as catalysts has the potential to improve already existing 
processes by being more cost-effective, sustainable and energy effective.   
Recently, the enzyme carbonic anhydrase (CA) has drawn attention as a biocatalyst for CO2 capture due to its 
ability to accelerate the hydration of carbon dioxide into bicarbonate. The combination of CA with an aqueous 
tertiary amine solution would preferably enhance the rate of CO2 absorption into the amine solution. 
This section focuses on the enzyme carbonic anhydrase and its ability to enhance CO2 absorption. The reaction 
mechanism and the Michaelis-Menten model will be explained in detail. 
4.1 Enzyme Fundamentals 
Enzymes are amino acid-based biological catalysts that are capable of accelerating reactions by a factor of 106 
or more [43]. The biochemical catalysis is taking place at the active site of the enzyme where the specific 
reactant, known as substrate, can bind.  
To understand how enzymes accelerate the rate of chemical reactions the following two thermodynamic 
properties are considered [43]. The first thermodynamic property is the free energy of change of a reaction ∆𝐺, 
which provides information about whether a reaction is spontaneous or not. This property however does not 
account for the rate of reaction. The second thermodynamic property is the energy needed to convert reactants 
into products. Enzymes accelerate reactions by lowering the activation energy between substrate and product 
so more molecules can enter the transition state [43]. The active site of the enzyme is responsible for the 
lowering of the activation energy. The active site consists of a three dimensional protein structure consisting 
of catalytic groups that participate in the breaking and making of bonds and is responsible for the enzyme’s 
specificity. The presence of a small molecule, called cofactor, is needed for many enzymes to be capable of 
performing chemical reactions. These cofactors can be divided into two groups: metals and coenzymes [43]. 
Enzyme-catalysed reactions can be divided into four different types such as: covalent-, acid-base-, metal ion 
catalysis or catalysis by approximation [43]. In covalent catalysis, the substrate is attached covalently to the 
active site. In acid-base catalysis, a molecule acts as a proton donor or acceptor. In metal ion catalysis, the 
metal ion acts catalytically on the formation of nucleophiles. Two substrates are brought together along a single 
binding surface in catalysis by approximation.  
4.2 Carbonic Anhydrase 
Carbonic anhydrase is a class of enzymes that catalyses the reversible CO2 hydration. The function of CA is 
to maintain the acid-base balance in e.g. blood and tissues. CA belongs to a broad group of zinc-containing 
enzymes that exist in five structurally different classes with the isoforms α, β, γ, δ and ɛ [49][50]. CA is found 
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 4 – Carbonic Anhydrase (CA) 
 
 
Page 14 of 134 
 
in prokaryotes, archaea and eukaryotes. α-CA is commonly found in mammals while β-CA dominates plants 
and algae. The γ class is found in the archea Methanosarcina thermophilia, which shows a different amino 
acid sequence than the α and β classes [49]. 
Meldrum et al. [51] discovered CA in cattle in 1933 while studying the transport of carbon dioxide in the 
blood. Observations suggested that the release of CO2 was faster than could be explained by physical mass 
transfer [51][52]. This triggered subsequent research on carbonic anhydrase have later been conducted. 11 
distinct α-CA isozymes are found in humans [50], where human CA type II (hCA-II), located in the red blood 
cells, is the most studied. Carbonic anhydrase is known to be one of the fastest enzymes hydrating 106 
molecules of CO2 per second per molecule of enzyme [43][52], which is why it could be very useful for the 
CO2 capture process.  
hCA-II contains a sequence of 260 amino acids [49][52]. The molecular weight of CA has been reported to be 
in the range 29.2 to 30 kDa [49][52][53]. hCA-II is considered to be a one-domain protein with a dominating 
structure consisting of a 10-stranded, twisted β-sheet illustrated in Figure 6 [54]. The protein chain of hCA-II 
is assumed to be folded into an nearly spherical shape [52][54]. The active center of hCA-II is located in a 
large cone-shaped cleft. The zinc(II) ion is situated in the bottom of the cavity and is linked to four ligands 
occupied by a water molecule and the histidine residues His94, His96 and His119 shown in Figure 7 [43][54].  
 
Figure 6: Structure of hCA-II. Large black dot is the zinc ion. 
Arrows indicate 𝛽-strands and ribbons indicate helices [54].  
Figure 7: The active center of hCA-II showing the zinc ion bound 
to four ligands [49].  
4.2.1 Mechanism of CA 
The zinc complex in carbonic anhydrase facilitates the hydration of carbon dioxide. CA employs three enzyme 
characteristics/mechanisms in the CO2 reaction. These mechanisms include catalysis by approximation, acid-
base- and metal ion catalysis. Catalysis by approximation is considered as CA brings the substrates CO2 and 
water together in order to rise the reaction rate. The histidine residue, His 64, in hCA-II serves as an H+ acceptor 
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 4 – Carbonic Anhydrase (CA) 
 
 
Page 15 of 134 
 
from the zinc-bound water molecule to form a hydroxide ion. The Zn2+ ion facilitates the formation of 
nucleophiles.   
The reaction mechanism for the conversion of CO2 to bicarbonate is illustrated in Figure 8. At step 1, a proton 
is removed from the zinc-bound water molecule by His64 to form a nucleophilic hydroxide ion and a 
protonated His64. CO2 is brought into position in the hydrophobic pocket near the active zinc hydroxide in 
step 2 [52]. In step 3, the zinc hydroxyl makes a nucleophile attack on the carbonyl carbon of CO2 producing 
zinc-bound bicarbonate. Bicarbonate is released to the solvent in step 4 and a water molecule binds to the zinc 
complex leaving CA inactive [43][52]. The regeneration of the enzyme activity is the rate limiting part of the 
mechanism and can be explained by a two-step mechanism with an intramolecular proton transfer to the proton 
channel (His64), and in the intermolecular proton transfer to a base in the bulk solvent [52]. Whether it is the 
intramolecular (high buffer concentration) or the intermolecular (low buffer concentration) proton transfer that 
is the rate limiting part depends on the buffer strength of the solvent [52]. The rate limiting part concerning 
industrial CO2 capture is the intermolecular proton transfer as the buffer concentration must be < 10 mM [33]. 
Concentrations up to 4 M for alkanolamine solvents are seen in the capture process.  
 
Figure 8: The four steps catalytic mechanism of CA [43][49]. Step 1: Deprotonation of water. Step 2: Binding of CO2. Step 3: 
Nucleophilic attack by hydroxide. Step 4: Displacement of bicarbonate.  
Depending on the chemical equilibrium, the enzyme catalyses either CO2 or bicarbonate. If the concentration 
of bicarbonate is low, carbon dioxide is consumed and the reverse occur if the bicarbonate concentration is 
high more CO2 will be produced. The overall reaction catalysed by CA, where B is the base, is expressed by: 
 
𝐶𝑂2 + 𝐻2𝑂+ 𝐵
𝐶𝐴
↔ 𝐻𝐶𝑂3
−+ 𝐵𝐻+ 
(xii) 
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 4 – Carbonic Anhydrase (CA) 
 
 
Page 16 of 134

(xiii). Furthermore, 𝐾𝑀 describes the CO2 concentration at which the maximum reaction rate is halved.

term is added to reaction (xiii). The inhibitor binds to the active site preventing the binding of carbon dioxide.

4.2.3 CA Catalysed CO2 Absorption 
CA’s ability to enhance the CO2 absorption rate and thereby facilitating separation of carbon dioxide from flue 
gas has made this enzyme interesting for many scientists. Alper et al. [56][57] studied the absorption of CO2 
in various buffer solutions with the addition of CA. They performed absorption experiments using a continuous 
stirred tank reactor (CSTR), a laminar jet and a wetted wall column. Based on their experiments they concluded 
that CA enhanced the absorption rate for all three apparatuses.  
Although CA is having a high catalytic activity, different drawbacks are associated such as limited tolerance 
to impurities, temperatures and pH. The presence of different species in the flue gas may inhibit the activity of 
the enzyme. In the review from Shekh et al. [58] the presence of 0.2 M Cl- and 50 mM of F-, I- and Br- ions 
inhibited the enzyme activity up to 50 %. While the presence of the metal ions Cd2+, Zn2+, CO2+ and Fe2+ were 
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 4 – Carbonic Anhydrase (CA) 
 
 
Page 18 of 134 
 
increasing the enzyme activity. The sensitivity of CA to the composition of flue gas was investigated by Bond 
et al. [59] and Lu et al. [48]. Both sources concluded that the presence of small concentrations of SOx, NOx 
and Cl- had a minor effect (less than 10 %) on the activity of CA.  
120 °C is a normal operating temperature for the CO2 regeneration process. At this temperature the stability of 
carbonic anhydrase decreases rapidly [60]. Burk [61] evaluated the use of CA for CO2 scrubbers for industrial 
application. It is explained that to ensure the stability of the enzyme a maximum temperature of 60 °C in the 
desorber unit must be obtained. Rossi et al. [62] investigated the use of the thermostable and thermoactive 
bacterium Sulfurihydrogenibium sp., which is stable up to 100 °C in aqueous solutions of K2CO3. Enhanced 
CO2 absorption rates were obtained.  
The solvents investigated in combination with CA for the CO2 capture technology have so far been focused on 
K2CO3 and MDEA. Lu et al. [48] studied the activity of carbonic anhydrase to promote CO2 absorption into 
aqueous potassium carbonate solutions using a stirred tank reactor (STR). For a CA dosage of 300 mg/L the 
absorption rate was enhanced 8.8 times at 25 °C compared to unpromoted K2CO3 solutions. Ye et al. [20] 
determined the rate constant of CA at 25, 40 and 50 °C in 20 wt% K2CO3-KHCO3 solutions with CO2 loadings. 
They concluded that the loading did not have an influence on the activity of CA, nor did the temperature. 
Zhang et al. [63] did also measure the reaction kinetics of CO2 absorption into CA promoted K2CO3 solution 
(20 wt%) using a STR. The CO2 loading did not affect the CA activity.  
Penders-van Elk et al. [30][39][61] have done several studies on CA with different solvents. In the first study 
[30], they present the CO2 absorption results of CA promoted MDEA solutions using a STR at 298 K. They 
found that the absorption rate was significantly enhanced compared to unpromoted MDEA solutions. 
Furthermore, they stated that there was an influence of water concentration on the reaction rate. In the second 
study by Penders-van Elk et al. [61] they were measuring the absorption kinetics of CA promoted Na2CO3 
solutions also by using a STR, but in the temperature range 298 to 333 K. This was in order to study the 
immobilizing of carbon anhydrase on particles. The results of the immobilization of CA on the particles 
resultated in a minor increase of the reaction kinetics compared to free enzymes in the solution. The same 
authors [39] also investigated the CA promoted amine solutions; diethylethanolamine (DEMEA), TEA, MEA 
and DMEA and they concluded that the enzymatic reaction rate increased with increasing pKa values of the 
amines.  
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 5 – Mass Transfer And Chemical Reaction Model 
 
 
Page 19 of 134 
 
5   Mass Transfer and Chemical Reaction Model  
The rate of CO2 absorption into CA promoted aqueous amine solutions is an important parameter for the sizing 
of the absorber column and it is therefore closely connected to the costs for the capturing process. In this thesis 
the CO2 absorption into the liquid solvent is performed with the wetted wall column (WWC) described by 
Darde et al. [27]. The WWC has previously been used for determination of mass transfer and kinetics of gas-
liquid reactions by several researchers [37][38][56][57] due to its simple design and the advantage of 
measuring the interfacial area accurately [64].  
In this section, the theory on which mass transfer of gas-liquid reactions is based, will be reviewed. 
5.1 Mass Transfer and Chemical Reaction Model  
Mass transfer, known as absorption or desorption depending in which way the transfer occur, between a gas 
and a liquid solvent can be explained by the two-film theory. The driving force for the mass transfer is the 
difference in chemical equilibria between the gas and the liquid phase.  
 
Figure 9: Schematic representation of the mass transfer from gas to liquid. 𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑔 is the bulk partial pressure of carbon dioxide in the 
vapour phase. 𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡 is the partial pressure at the gas-liquid interface and 𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑙 is the partial pressure of  the bulk liquid. 𝑘𝑔 and 𝑘𝑙
′ 
are the gas and liquid side mass transfer coefficients [19][65].  
Based on the two-film theory shown in Figure 9, the gas and the liquid phases can be divided into two stagnant 
films with a thickness of 𝛿 and in-between an interface where the mass transport takes place. The interface is 
considered to be in a thermodynamic equilibrium. Due to turbulent flow, it is assumed that the concentration 
is constant in the liquid bulk. The flow in the film layer is considered laminar. If absorption occurs, the 
concentration through the gas side to the liquid side is decreasing [66][67]. The mass transfer in the gas and 
the liquid phases are limited by resistances, which are described by the film thickness and the diffusion 
coefficient 𝐷.  
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 5 – Mass Transfer And Chemical Reaction Model 
 
 
Page 20 of 134 
 
The molar flux of carbon dioxide 𝑁𝐶𝑂2expressed in mol/(m2∙s) through the gas and the liquid films, can be 
described by Fick’s first law, which states that the flux is proportional to the driving force [27]. 
The transfer of carbon dioxide into the liquid as depicted in Figure 9 can be described in two ways. First, as 
the flux of CO2 from the bulk gas to the gas film: 
 
𝑁𝐶𝑂2,𝑔= 𝑘𝑔(𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑔−𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡) 
(20) 
where 𝑘𝑔 is the gas side mass transfer coefficient expressed in mol/(m2∙s∙Pa). 𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑔 and 𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡 represent the 
driving force in terms of the partial pressure of CO2 at the bulk gas and at the interface respectively in Pa. The 
second way of describing the flux is as the flux across the interface to the liquid bulk. The driving force is here 
expressed in terms of CO2 concentration (mol/m3). 
 
𝑁𝐶𝑂2,𝑙= 𝑘𝑙(𝐶𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡−𝐶𝐶𝑂2,𝑙) 
(21) 
The liquid side mass transfer coefficient in chemical absorption processes 𝑘𝑙 can be described by the physical 
mass transfer coefficient 𝑘𝑙
0 in the liquid and the enhancement factor 𝐸𝐶𝑂2. 𝑘𝑙 and 𝑘𝑙
0 are expressed in m/s.

5.2 Absorption modelling  
The enhancement factor described previously can be solved by introducing the Hatta number (Ha) [27][65]. 
The Hatta number compares the rate of reaction into the liquid film to the rate of diffusion through the film 
and is defined by: 
 
𝐻𝑎= √𝑘𝑜𝑣𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴
𝑘𝑙
0
 
(32) 
where 𝑘𝑜𝑣 is the overall kinetic rate constant and 𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴 is the diffusion coefficient of CO2 into MDEA 
expressed respectively in 1/s and m2/s. The diffusivity of carbon dioxide in MDEA solutions was described by 
Al-Ghawas et al. [68], which used the N2O analogy to estimate the diffusion parameters. Based on their 
experimental work they developed a correlated form of the Stokes-Einstein relation to describe the diffusion: 
 
𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴⋅𝜇𝐾18 = 𝐾19 
(33) 
with 𝜇 being the solution viscosity in cP. 𝐾18 and 𝐾19 are parameters obtained from equation (27). Equation 
(33) was further simplified: 
 
𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴= 𝐾4 + 𝐾5𝑇+ 𝐾6𝑇2 
(34) 
where 𝐾4, 𝐾5 and 𝐾6 are expressed by the correlation obtained in equation (27) and 𝑘𝑖,𝑗 can be found in Table 
14 in appendix A. The correlation does not account for CO2 loading.

The reaction order of MDEA is of second order, which according to Darde et al. [27] makes the determination 
of the enhancement factor difficult. If it is assumed that MDEA is not depleted in the interface, the condition 
can be defined as a pseudo first order reaction. This means that the concentration of MDEA is much greater 
than the concentration of CO2 and the amine concentration is constant through the liquid film. Kucka et al. [70] 
reported the condition for a pseudo first order reaction: 
 
2 < 𝐻𝑎≪𝐸∞ 
(35) 
where 𝐸∞ is the enhancement factor for an instantaneous reaction. At this condition, CO2 is considered 
depleted in the solvent and 𝐸∞ can be defined as:  
 
𝐸∞= 1 +
𝐷𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴,𝑙𝐶𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴,𝑙
𝛾𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴𝐶𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡
 
(36) 
with 𝐷𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴,𝑙 (m2/s) and 𝐶𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴,𝑏 (mol/m3) being the diffusion coefficient and the concentration of MDEA in 
the bulk liquid respectively. 𝛾 is the stoichiometric coefficient of MDEA. 𝐶𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡 (mol/m3) is the concentration 
of carbon dioxide at the gas-liquid interface and 𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴 (m2/s) is the diffusion coefficient of CO2 into 
MDEA. If the pseudo first order reaction is applied, the enhancement factor for the absorption of CO2 becomes:  
 
𝐸= √𝑘𝑜𝑣𝐷𝐶𝑂2,𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴

5.3 Physical mass transfer coefficient 
The physical mass transfer coefficient is determined using the same approach as Pacheco [71]. The following 
assumptions in the model are made: 
 
Parabolic velocity profile in the liquid phase 
 
Concentration of the diffusing species in the interface is uniform 
The convective transport is considered in the direction of the flow and the diffusive transport is perpendicular 
to the flow direction. The model is based on the change in the CO2 concentration (𝜃) described by: 
 
𝜃= 𝐶𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛𝑡−𝐶𝐶𝑂2,𝑜𝑢𝑡𝑙𝑒𝑡

6   Extended UNIQUAC 
In this thesis, the apparent Henry coefficient and the bicarbonate concentration are derived from the 
thermodynamic model, Extended UNIQUAC. The used model is an in-house model, which works with Excel. 
The use of a thermodynamic model allows simulating all the compounds in the solution at different CO2 
loading, temperature and MDEA concentrations. It was assumed that the addition of CA had no influence on 
the thermodynamic properties. The parameters used in the model has been fitted on experimental data by 
Sadegh et al. [73]. Extended UNIQUAC is an activity coefficient model consisting of a combinatorial 
(entropic), a residual (enthalpic) and an electrostatic term defined by [74][75]:

Chapter 7 – Material and Experimental Procedure 
 
 
Page 26 of 134 
 
7   Materials and Experimental Procedure 
This chapter covers a description of the materials used for the experiments and a description of the wetted wall 
column. The experimental procedure for running the WWC is provided and methods for determining the CO2 
loading, density and viscosity in the solvent are furthermore described.  
7.1 Overview of experiments 
The absorption kinetics are determined for MDEA solutions with and without CA. Experiments are conducted 
at three different temperatures and for CO2 loadings up to 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent. An overview of the 
experiments is shown in Table 4. Parallel to the absorption/desorption experiments on the wetted wall column, 
physical properties as density and viscosity of the solvents are determined in order to convert the experimental 
results into kinetic rate constants. 
Table 4: Overview of experiments. 
Solvent 
T (K) 
CO2 loading 
30 wt% MDEA 
298, 313, 328 
0 to 0.5 
30 wt% MDEA + 0.15 wt% CA 
298, 313, 328 
0 to 0.5 
30 wt% MDEA + 0.3 wt% CA 
298, 313, 328 
0 to 0.5 
15 wt% MDEA + 0.15 wt% CA 
298, 313, 328 
0 to 0.5 
50 wt% MDEA + 0.15 wt% CA 
298, 313, 328 
0 to 0.5 
 
7.2 Materials 
N,N-dimethylethanolamine (MDEA) with a purity of  ≥ 99 % was purchased from Sigma-Aldrich. MDEA was 
diluted with demineralized water to obtain the desired mass fractions of the solution. Novozymes A\S provided 
the enzyme, carbonic anhydrase (CA). CA was diluted in the solvent to obtain concentrations of either 0.15 or 
0.3 wt%. N2 and CO2 used for the absorption and desorption experiments were supplied from AGA both with 
purities of 99.99 %. 
For the determination of the liquid content of CO2 in the solvents, standard solutions of HCl and NaOH were 
obtained from Fluka with concentrations of 0.5 M and 0.1 M respectively. A 0.5 M solution of BaCl2 was 
made from barium chloride dihydrate (≥ 99 %, Sigma-Aldrich) and dissolved in demineralized water. 
Precipitates were filtered on 0.45 µm nitrocellulose membrane filters purchased from Millipore.  
For cleaning between runs demineralised water and ethanol (purity 96 vol%), which was obtained from VWR, 
were used. 
A chemical risk assessment is available in Appendix B. 
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 7 – Material and Experimental Procedure

7.3 Experimental setups 
7.3.1 Description of the WWC setup 
The rate of CO2 absorption into aqueous MDEA with and without CA was determined with the WWC. The 
WWC was designed by Victor Darde [27][65] for his Ph.D. study and later modified by Ph.D. student Arne 
Gladis. Figure 10 shows an overview of the apparatus where the liquid solvent is in counter-current contact 
with the gas mixture. The setup consists of a liquid-, a gas- and a water system represented by blue, red and 
black respectively in the figure.  
 
Figure 10: Experimental setup. Black stream indicates the water system. Blue and red streams indicate the liquid and gas systems 
respectively [72]. 
In the liquid system (marked with blue in Figure 10), the solvent is led into a liquid reservoir that is closed 
during experiments. The solvent is pumped through a Sho-Rate flowmeter. The liquid flow rate can be adjusted 
by the pump. The solvent is further pumped through a heat exchanger, which is built into a water bath, to 
ensure a correct temperature when entering the wetted wall column in the bottom. The liquid is rising inside 
the stainless steel tube and flows down on the outside of the tube forming a liquid film. The liquid that is 
outside the stainless steel tube is recycled into the liquid reservoir. A Pt100 probe (T3) measures the 
temperature of the solvent temperature when leaving the WWC.  
In the gas system (marked with red in Figure 10), N2 and CO2 are mixed by the Bronkhorst mass flow 
controllers before the gas flows through the WWC. The gas is led through two water saturators: a pre-saturator 
at room temperature and a saturator placed in a temperature controlled water bath. The saturated gas enters the 
reaction chamber from three gas inlets at the bottom. The gas can either be led through the WWC or bypass 
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 7 – Material and Experimental Procedure 
 
 
Page 28 of 134 
 
mode. After the gas has left the WWC (or the bypass mode) it is led through a condenser. Leaving the 
condenser, the pressure (Pi) and the temperature (T1) of the gas are recorded. An NDIR CO2 probe measures 
the concentration of CO2.  
To maintain a constant temperature in the reaction chamber, a glass jacket encloses the chamber where water 
from the water bath is led through. A constant temperature of 288K is maintained in the condenser by 
circulating water from a second water bath. The water streams are indicated in black in Figure 10. A heat gun 
is used to heat up the gas and surroundings for temperatures above 298 K. 
The dimensions of the reaction chamber were described in detail by Darde et al. [27] and are outlined in Figure 
11. The liquid reservoir contains 0.7 L. Temperatures, pressure and CO2 concentration are recorded with the 
Datalogger software and read on the PC. Mass controllers, temperature-, pressure- and CO2 probes have been 
calibrated by Ph.D. student Arne Gladis.  
 
Figure 11: A close-up of the reaction chamber with the dimensions of the stainless steel tube and outer glass tube. Water, gas and 
liquid systems are represented by black red and blue. 
To extract the reaction kinetics from WWC experiments a simple mole balance is used to determine the 
absorbed molar flux of CO2 in the reaction chamber. The mole balance is based on inert gas, which contains 
N2 and H2O and the contact area of the gas-liquid interface 𝐴 (m2) of the WWC. The molar flux of absorbed 
carbon dioxide ∆𝑁𝐶𝑂2 is: 
 
∆𝑁𝐶𝑂2 =
𝑁𝑖𝑛𝑒𝑟𝑡(𝑦𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛−𝑦𝐶𝑂2,𝑜𝑢𝑡)
(1 −𝑦𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛) ⋅(1 −𝑦𝐶𝑂2,𝑜𝑢𝑡)
𝐴
 
(60) 
where 𝑁𝑖𝑛𝑒𝑟𝑡 is the saturated inert gas flow in mol/s. The mole fractions 𝑦𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛 and  𝑦𝐶𝑂2,𝑜𝑢𝑡 are carbon dioxide 
measured in the bypass mode and after leaving the gas through the reaction chamber.

the partial pressure of CO2 [27][45][65]:  
 
𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑔= 𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛−𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑜𝑢𝑡

𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑖𝑛 is the partial pressure in the inlet of the chamber and is measured when the chamber is bypassed. 
𝑝𝐶𝑂2,𝑜𝑢𝑡 is the partial pressure measured when the gas is running through the reaction chamber [27]. The log 
mean average of the partial pressure of CO2 is applied in order to simplify the determination of the reaction 
kinetics. Applying linear regression 𝐾𝑜𝑣 can be obtained as the slope of the line [27][45][65]. The liquid bulk 
partial pressure of carbon dioxide is obtained when the flux is zero as no absorption or desorption occurs.

7.3.2 Viscometer 
The dynamic viscosity of the solvents were analysed with a rotating ball micro viscometer Paar AMV 200 
(Anton Paar) as depicted in Figure 12. The capillary, containing sample and a ball with a known density is 
placed in a thermostatic block (A). Temperature and detection of the time of the rolling ball are measured by 
a temperature sensor and by two magnetic sensors inside the block. Communication with the instrument occurs 
via the keyboard and the display (B). For analysis of the viscosity in the range 0.3 to 10 mPa∙s, a capillary with 
an inner diameter of 1.6 mm and balls with the density of 7.65 g/cm3 were used. Capillary and balls are 
presented in Figure 13. The accuracy of the viscometer is ± 0.01 mPa∙s. 
 
Figure 12 - Rotating ball micro viscometer Paar AMV 200. A: 
Thermostatic capillary block, B: Keyboard and display. 
 
Figure 13 - A: Ball with known density, B: Glass capillary, C: 
Filling adapter, D: Lids. 
7.3.3 Density meter 
The density was measured with the density meter DMA from Anton Paar. The apparatus is shown in Figure 
14. The apparatus is installed with an oscillating U-shaped glass tube which based on the measured electronic 
frequency of the oscillation, ensures accurate measurements of the density. The uncertainty of the instrument 
is ± 0.0001 g/cm3. 
 
Figure 14 – Density meter from Aton Paar.

7.4 Experimental methods 
The absorption experiments were carried out with aqueous solutions of MDEA with and without CA. All 
solutions where prepared on weight basis for accuracy. It is important that pH is below 11.2 in order to have a 
stable enzyme when preparing the MDEA solutions containing CA. To reduce pH, small amounts of CO2 are 
bubbled into the MDEA solvent before adding the enzyme.  
The absorption of CO2 into the solvents were measured on the wetted wall column. In addition, CO2 loading, 
density and viscosity were determined. This section explains the experimental procedure of the WWC, the 
content of CO2 in the solvent, density and viscosity analysis. 
7.4.1 WWC procedure 
Before starting up an experiment, pre-saturator and saturator were filled with demineralised water (approx. 
400 g each) and connected to the gas system (marked in red, Figure 10). The gas system was set on bypass 
mode and the end valve was open in order to ensure that gas could escape. Afterwards the gas bottles could be 
opened. The flow controllers and datalogger system were switched on the computer and a N2 gas flow of 3.0 
nL/min was let through the gas loop. The set point temperature on the water bath was between 0.5 to 4 K above 
the reaction temperature. The set point temperatures of the water bath are listed in Table 5. 
Table 5: Reaction temperatures versus set point temperatures for the water bath. 
Reaction temperature (K) 
Temperature in water bath (K) 
298 
298.5 
313 
315 
328 
332 
For reaction temperatures above 298 K, a heat gun was used to heat up the gas stream and prevent heat loss 
from the piping. In order to fill the liquid reservoir, the pipe between the pump and the WWC was detached 
and the valve on top of the reservoir was open. By adjusting the speed on the pump, the solvent was pumped 
into the liquid reservoir, which had a total volume of 0.7 L. When the reservoir was filled, the pump was 
stopped, the top valve was closed, and the pipe between the pump and WWC was connected. Then the solvent 
could slowly be pumped through the WWC in the bypass mode. It was ensured that the liquid film was uniform 
and stable as the solvent was flowing down the stainless steel tube. 
The entire system was equilibrated for approximately 30 to 45 min to make sure that the liquid and the gas 
temperatures entering the wetted wall column were at the desired absorption temperature.  
For an experimental run, the gas stream was changed to include CO2. The total gas stream was set to 3.0 
nL/min, which meant that the N2 content decreased whit increasing the CO2 content. An experimental run 
consisted of the following: 
 
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 7 – Material and Experimental Procedure 
 
 
Page 32 of 134 
 
 
System was set on bypass  
 
Set a desired amount of CO2 
 
Obtain steady state (approx. 30 min)  
 
Switch to the WWC and wait for steady state (approx. 15 min) 
 
Note the liquid flow by reading the flow meter 
 
Switch back to bypass and note pressure, temperatures and CO2 concentration for bypass and the 
WWC 
This procedure was repeated four to six times with different amounts of CO2 in order to make a calibration 
curve at the reaction temperatures 298, 313 and 328 K. After a run, the solvent was loaded with carbon dioxide. 
This was done by switching off the N2 mass flow controller and injecting CO2 at a flow rate of 0.2 nL/min. It 
was decided to evaluate the solvents at four to five CO2 loadings. After each loading, the system was flushed 
with nitrogen to remove CO2 in pipes and reaction chamber.  
Changing to a new solvent, the WWC was cleaned with approximately 10 L of demineralised water and dried 
with pressurised air.  
7.4.2 Analysis of CO2 Content in Liquid Phase (BaCl2 method) 
For the determination of the CO2 content in the liquid the method described by Sun et al. [76] and Sønderby 
et al. [77] was used. A liquid sample is withdrawn from the WWC after each loading and is mixed with an 
excess of 0.1 M NaOH resulting in the formation of bicarbonate and carbonate. An excess of 0.5 M BaCl2 is 
added to the solution, which permit HCO3- and CO32- to precipitate as BaCO3. Barium carbonate is filtered and 
dissolved with 0.5 M HCl. The addition of HCl allows for the release of CO2. The solution is titrated with 0.1 
M NaOH. The amount of CO2 in weight percentage is determined as the difference in excess HCl compared 
to NaOH expressed in equation (67) [77].  
 
𝑤𝑡% 𝐶𝑂2 = 𝐶𝐻𝐶𝑙𝑉𝐻𝐶𝑙−𝐶𝑁𝑎𝑂𝐻𝑉𝑁𝑎𝑂𝐻
2𝑚𝑠𝑎𝑚𝑝𝑙𝑒
⋅𝑀𝐶𝑂2 ⋅100% 
(67) 
To evaluate the loading, defined as the ratio of mole CO2 and solvent (equation (68)), it must be accounted for 
that the solvent is diluted due to dissolved carbon dioxide. The actual weight percentage of the solvent when 
CO2 is diluted in the solution is expressed in equation (69). 
 
𝛼𝐶𝑂2 =
𝑛𝐶𝑂2
𝑛𝑠𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑛𝑡
=
𝑤𝑡% 𝐶𝑂2
𝑤𝑡% 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑛𝑡
𝑀𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑛𝑡
𝑀𝐶𝑂2
 
(68) 
 
𝑤𝑡% 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑛𝑡= 100 −𝑤𝑡% 𝐶𝑂2
100
⋅𝑤𝑡% 𝑆𝑜𝑙𝑣𝑒𝑛𝑡 (𝑆𝑡𝑎𝑟𝑡) 
(69) 
The procedure for the liquid phase analysis is as follows. The weight of a beaker is noted and 5 ml of 0.1 M 
NaOH is added and the total weight is noted again. Depending on the expected loading, between 1.0 to 1.5 g 
of sample is added to the beaker and the total weight is noted. 25 ml of 0.5 M BaCl2 is transferred to the beaker. 
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 7 – Material and Experimental Procedure 
 
 
Page 33 of 134 
 
The solution is heated to the boiling point and left to boil for approx. 4 min. Afterwards the solution is cooled 
to room temperature before it can be vacuum filtered using a 45 µm cellulose nitrate filter. Filter and beaker 
are washed with 100 ml of demineralised water. The weight of the empty beaker was noted and 12 ml of 0.5 
M HCl is added and the weight noted. The filter is put back to the beaker and 20 ml of demineralised water is 
added. To make sure that all particles are dissolved the solution is boiled for 4 min. The solution is titrated 
with 0.1 M NaOH to pH 8 using a Metrohm autotitrator.  
A scale with an accuracy of ± 0.01 g was used to measure the weights of the samples and chemicals. The CO2 
loading in each sample was determined three times and the experimental error was less than 0.04 mol CO2/mol 
solvent. An example of a titration report and raw data are presented in appendix C. 
7.4.3 Viscosity analysis 
The apparatus is calibrated with demineralized water, as the physical properties are well known. The 
calibration constant for each inclination angle used for the measurements of the samples is determined at 298, 
313 and 328 K. The angles ranging from 20° to 65° with a stepwise increase of 5°. The calibration constant 
𝐾(𝛼) is determined from equation (70).  
 
𝐾(𝛼) =
𝜂𝐻2𝑂
𝑡⋅(𝜌𝑏𝑎𝑙𝑙− 𝜌𝑙𝑖𝑞𝑢𝑖𝑑)     
(70) 
where 𝑡 is the rolling time expressed in seconds, 𝜂𝐻2𝑂 and 𝜌𝑙𝑖𝑞𝑢𝑖𝑑 are the viscosity and density obtained from 
literature. 𝜌𝑏𝑎𝑙𝑙 is the density of the ball. 𝜂𝐻2𝑂 and the densities are expressed in mPa∙s and g/cm3 respectively. 
The viscosity of the samples is evaluated from expression (71). 
 
𝜂= 𝐾(𝛼) ⋅𝑡⋅(𝜌𝑏𝑎𝑙𝑙− 𝜌𝑙𝑖𝑞𝑢𝑖𝑑)     
(71) 
The procedure for the determination of the viscosity is as follows: a ball with a known density is added to the 
capillary and afterwards the liquid sample is filled into the top of the tube using a syringe. Liquid is added until 
a drop is seen on the open end of the tube. Both ends are then closed. It is made sure that the liquid inside the 
capillary tube is free of air bubbles, as this would interfere with the measurement. The system is stabilised for 
180 s before it measures the viscosity at different angles. For cleaning of the capillary, demineralised water 
and ethanol are used. The tube is afterwards dried with dry air.  
7.4.4 Density analysis 
The density meter is calibrated using demineralised water at the temperatures 298, 313 and 328 K. The sample 
is filled into the U-tube. It is made sure that no bubbles are interfering the measurement. After equilibration 
the result is shown on the display. To avoid vaporisation of the samples at the temperatures above 298 K, the 
outlet tube was closed with a clamp. Before each measurement the instrument was cleaned with demineralised 
water and ethanol. A water sample was analysed each time and compared to literature in order to ensure that 
no impurities were influencing the measurement. For accuracy, the measurements were conducted in 
triplicates.  
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 8 – Results and Discussion 
 
 
Page 34 of 134 
 
8   Results and Discussion 
Results and discussions of the experimental work are presented in this section.  
8.1 Density and Viscosity 
Density and viscosity are important physical properties for the absorption and desorption of carbon dioxide 
into aqueous MDEA solutions. These properties are especially important for the modelling of the gas side mass 
transfer coefficient as density and viscosity are linked to the determination of the film thickness and the 
diffusion coefficient. Currently, there is no information available of the effect of CO2 loading on the density 
and viscosity of aqueous MDEA solutions containing carbonic anhydrase.  
The influence of adding CA to MDEA as well as the effect of CO2 loading on the solvent were investigated at 
the temperatures 298, 313 and 328 K and with CO2 loadings from 0 to 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent. The BaCl2 
method was used to determine the CO2 loading. The results of density and viscosity are presented in Table 20, 
Table 21 and Table 22 in appendix D.1. The uncertainty of the density apparatus is ± 0.0001 g/cm3. The 
measurements were conducted in triplicates with no standard deviations. A maximum standard deviation of 
0.1 mPa∙s was obtained for the viscosity measurements. Either particles in the solution or small bubbles may 
have caused the high deviation in the viscosity measurements.  
The measured density at different CO2 loadings at 298, 313 and 328 K is shown in Figure 15. The figure depicts 
increasing densities at increasing CO2 loadings and decreasing densities at increasing temperatures.  
Comparing the density of 30 wt% MDEA with 0.3 wt% CA to the density of 30 wt% MDEA at 298 K the 
density seems to be decreasing. Temperature and MDEA concentration appear to have less effect on the 
densities whereas the loading of CO2 contributes to a significant increase. At 328 K and a CO2 loading of 
approximately 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent, desorption was observed in the density apparatus in the form of 
bubbles, which made it impossible to measure the density.

The measured viscosities of 15, 30 and 50 wt% MDEA solutions with CA concentrations from 0 to 0.3 wt% 
versus CO2 loading are presented in Figure 16. The viscosity of  30 wt% MDEA solution was validated against 
the correlation proposed by Weiland et al. [78]. The correlation is presented in appendix D.2. The measured 
data are in good agreement with the correlation. It was observed that the addition of CA to MDEA, seemed to 
have an increasing effect on the viscosity. CO2 loading has a minor influence on the viscosity, which is 
illustrated in the figure. The behaviour of the viscosity versus loading is almost constant and only an 
insignificant decrease is observed at high CO2 loading.  
The viscosity is linked to the determination of the diffusion coefficient of CO2 in MDEA. The estimation of 
the diffusion coefficient is based on the N2O analogy, which in this case does not account for the CO2 loading 
in the solvent. As the viscosity is not changing with CO2 loading, it justifies to use the N2O analogy without 
accounting for loading.  
Temperature and concentration however have a significant influence on the viscosity, which can be seen by 
decreasing viscosities with increasing temperature and decreasing concentration. At 328 K and a loading of 
0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent desorption was observed during the experiment. Desorption in the form of bubbles 
influenced the rolling ball and viscosity could therefore not be measured.

8.1.1 Density Correlation 
In order to reduce the amount of experimental work, a model was developed. Therefore the density correlation 
proposed by Weiland et al. [78] was modified by adding an extra term for the enzyme. The density (g/cm3) of 
the aqueous solution of MDEA with CA is described by: 
 
𝜌= 𝑥𝐶𝐴⋅𝑀𝐶𝐴+ 𝑥𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴𝑀𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴+ 𝑥𝐻2𝑂𝑀𝐻2𝑂+ 𝑥𝐶𝑂2𝑀𝐶𝑂2
𝑉
 
(72) 
where the average molecular weight is defined by the molecular weight (𝑀𝑖) in g/mol and the mole fractions 
(𝑥𝑖) of the species carbonic anhydrase, MDEA, water and CO2 respectively. 𝑉 is the total molar volume of the 
solvent including the pure component and excess molar volumes expressed in cm3/mol. The total molar volume 
is: 
 
𝑉= 𝑥𝐶𝐴⋅𝑉𝐶𝐴+ 𝑥𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴𝑉𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴+ 𝑥𝐻2𝑂𝑉𝐻2𝑂+ 𝑥𝐶𝑂2𝑉𝐶𝑂2 + 𝑥𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴𝑥𝐻2𝑂𝑉∗+ 𝑥𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴𝑥𝐶𝑂2𝑉∗∗     (73) 
The expressions of the molar volume of MDEA, the molar volume of the interaction between MDEA and 
water, 𝑉∗ and the molar volume of the interaction between CO2 and MDEA, 𝑉∗∗ were described by Weiland 
et al. [78]. 
 
𝑉𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴=
𝑀𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴
𝑎𝑇2+𝑏𝑇+𝑐  and  𝑉∗∗= 𝑑+ 𝑒𝑥𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴    
(74) 
with 𝑇 being the temperature in K and 𝑎, 𝑏, 𝑐, 𝑑 and 𝑒 are parameters used to fit the density. The correlation of 
the molar volume of water, 𝑉𝐻2𝑂, is presented in appendix D.3. An expression for the molar volume of CA, 
𝑉𝐶𝐴 was derived from the crystal structure of hCA-II. The parameters used to express the molar volume were 
determined by Robbins et al. [79], who studied the doubled monoclinic structure of hCA-II. The molar volume 
of carbonic anhydrase can be expressed by:

To validate the proposed density correlation, the predicted values are compared to the measured values 
depicted in Figure 17. The correlation is validated for MDEA concentrations ranging from 15 to 50 wt% with 
CA concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 wt% at 298 to 328 K and with CO2 loadings from 0 to 0.5 mol CO2/mol 
solvent. If the correlation is accurate, the results should be laying on a straight line. As shown in the figure, 
the predicted densities are in agreement with the measured densities within an average of ± 1 %. Outliers are 
observed above 1.08 g/cm3 for a MDEA concentration of 30 wt%, which is leading to an error of 1.5 %.

328 K. Dashed lines show the 1 % deviation range.Calculated densities are derived from equation (72). 
The solvents 15 and 30 wt% MDEA with 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA respectively are further validated. The 
calculated densities and the experimental values at the temperatures 298, 313 and 328 K versus the CO2 
loadings are presented in Figure 18. The correlation as well as the measured values both indicate the same 
trend that density is increasing with increasing CO2 loading. The correlation is deviating at a CO2 loading 
above 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent. A 30 wt% MDEA solution with 0.3 wt% CA was compared to 30 wt% MDEA. 
For 30 wt% MDEA, the measured values were evaluated against the correlation proposed by Weiland et al. 
[78] (appendix D.4). The measured data are in good agreement with the correlation proposed by Weiland et 
al. [78]. The correlation proposed in this work shows that for unloaded solutions, the density is decreased by 
0.5 % by adding CA in the aqueous MDEA solutions compared to MDEA solutions without the enzyme. This 
is in line with the observation seen for the experimental values. Temperature and concentration appear to have 
a minor effect on the density compared to the CO2 loadings.

The correlation, equation (72), can be used to estimate the densities of MDEA solutions containing CA with 
CO2 loadings up to 0.5 mol of CO2/mol of MDEA. At higher loadings, the error is high. The equation is valid 
in the temperature range 298 to 328 K and with MDEA concentration from 15 to 50 wt% and enzyme 
concentrations up to 0.3 wt% with an average prediction of ± 1 %.  
Comparing both figures, good agreement exists between the calculated and the experimental values. The 
density correlation accounts for the addition of carbonic anhydrase in aqueous MDEA solutions relatively well 
and the correlation is therefore assumed accurate.  
8.1.2 Viscosity Correlation 
A viscosity correlation, which can predict the unloaded and the CO2 loaded MDEA solutions containing CA 
was developed. The correlation was inspired by the linear viscosity correlation for mixed amine solutions 
proposed by Weiland et al. [78], which involve the mass fractions and the viscosities of single amine 
compounds in water. In this study, the expression of the viscosity of MDEA containing CA is expressed by: 
 
𝜂𝑚𝑖𝑥,𝛼=
𝑤𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴
𝑤𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴+ 𝑤𝐶𝐴
𝜂𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴+
𝑤𝐶𝐴
𝑤𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴+ 𝑤𝐶𝐴
𝜂𝐶𝐴 
(76) 
where 𝜂𝑚𝑖𝑥,𝛼 is the viscosity of the aqueous MDEA solution containing CA. 𝜂𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴 and 𝜂𝐶𝐴 are the viscosities 
of MDEA and CA respectively in water and in 0.1 M NaOH at the weight fractions 𝑤𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴 and 𝑤𝐶𝐴. 𝛼 is 
represented by the CO2 loading. For the determination of the viscosity of CA, carbonic anhydrase was mixed 
with NaOH (0.1 M) instead of water as it was observed that the enzyme precipitated, which is depicted in 
Figure 19.  
 
Figure 19: Dilution series of CA in water. The white precipitates in the bottom of the vials are carbonic anhydrase. 
A more diluted NaOH solution (0.05 M) was also considered, but precipitation occurred. 0.1 M NaOH solution 
was chosen, as density and viscosity are similar to the properties of water. Besides, pH is in the same range as 
the MDEA solutions. A linear dependency between temperature and viscosity of CA was found to describe 
the viscosity best. The viscosity of CA was correlated by: 
 
𝜂𝐶𝐴= 𝑎𝑇+ 𝑏 
(77) 
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 8 – Results and Discussion 
 
 
Page 42 of 134 
 
where 𝑎 and 𝑏 are coefficients listed in Table 7 and 𝑇 is the temperature in K. Further information about the 
correlation of CA in 0.1 M NaOH is available in appendix D.5. 
Table 7: Parameters used to fit equation (77). The parameters is valid in the temperature range 298 to 328 K.

The calculated viscosities were plotted versus the measured viscosities in order to validate equation (76). The 
plot is depicted in Figure 20, where the dashed lines indicate the deviation range from 0 to 10 %. The predicted 
viscosities based on equation (76) agree with the measured values within ± 10 %. An outlier is observed in 50 
wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA at 298 K, which is leading to an error of 20 %. Equation (76) is clearly 
underestimating the viscosities and the equation is therefore not considered valid in predicting the viscosities 
of aqueous MDEA solutions containing enzyme. 
 
Figure 20: Predicted versus measured viscosity in 15 to 50 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 wt% at 298, 313 and 
328 K. Dashed lines show the deviation range from 0 to 10 %. Calculated viscosities are derived from equation (76). 
Another approach was considered in order to make a suitable prediction of the viscosity of CA promoted 
MDEA solvents. Based on Lindskog [54] and Salmon et al. [52] it was assumed that CA forms spherical 
particles. As the enzyme is very dilute in the solvent, the Einstein equation for the viscosity of a dilute 
suspension of spherical particles was chosen [80]: 
 
𝜂𝑚𝑖𝑥,𝛼= 𝜂0 (1 −5

where 𝜂0 is the viscosity of MDEA expressed in mPa∙s and 𝜙 is the volume fraction of particles in the solution. 
The viscosity of MDEA is calculated from the correlation proposed by Weiland et al. [78]. The volume fraction 
was fitted using the least squares approach by minimizing the objective function:   
 
𝑆= ∑(𝜂𝑚𝑖𝑥,𝛼
𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠𝑢𝑟𝑒𝑑−𝜂𝑚𝑖𝑥,𝛼

2
𝑖
 
(79) 
with 𝑖 being the corresponding measured and predicted values. The minimization was performed with the 
Excel solver add-in. 𝜙 resulted in values of 0.75∙10-2 and 1.51∙10-2 for 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA respectively. 
Applying linear regression for the volume fraction and the CA concentration, the following the expression was 
obtained: 
 
𝜙= 5 ⋅10−2 ⋅𝐶𝐶𝐴(𝑤𝑡%) 
(80) 
The correlation was compared to the measured viscosity of 15 to 50 wt% MDEA solvents with CA 
concentrations of respectively 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA at different CO2 loading and at 298, 313 and 328 K. The 
calculated values as a function of the measured viscosities are shown in Figure 21. The dashed lines are the 
deviation ranging from 0 to 10 %. By the use of the Einstein equation (78), it is possible to predict the effect 
of CO2 loading on the viscosity of CA promoted MDEA solvents within an average uncertainty of ± 4 %. 
Deviations greater than 10 % are seen for 50 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA at 298 K. The reproducibility of 
the measurements is limited as a maximum standard deviation of 0.1 mPa∙s was obtained in 50 wt% MDEA 
solutions with CA. The prediction is clearly improved compared with the correlation based on mass fraction 
weighting in equation (76).

8.2 Overall Mass Transfer Coefficient 
The liquid side mass transfer coefficient 𝑘𝑙
′ is determined from the overall mass transfer coefficient, 𝐾𝑜𝑣 and 
from the gas side mass transfer coefficient 𝑘𝑔 using equation (31). A calculation example and plots of the 
determination of 𝐾𝑜𝑣 in the solvents are found in appendix E and mass transfer coefficients are found en 
appendix F. 𝑘𝑔 is modelled by knowing the dimensions of the WWC and the gas properties. 𝐾𝑜𝑣 is 
experimentally determined from measurements of the CO2 flux at a certain driving force. The CO2 flux and 
the mean logarithmic partial pressure of CO2 were obtained from equations (60) and (66) respectively. It was 
expected that there would be a linear correlation between the flux and the partial pressure with the overall mass 
transfer coefficient as the slope of the curve. 
The overall mass transfer coefficient was measured in MDEA concentrations from 15 to 50 wt% and with CA 
concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 wt%. 𝐾𝑜𝑣 was also measured in 30 wt% MDEA without the enzyme. 
Measurements were carried out at 298, 313 and 328 K and with increasing CO2 loading. An example of the 
unloaded MDEA solvents with and without enzyme at 298 K is depicted in Figure 24. Four to six CO2 partial 
pressures were tested for each solvent in order to make a calibration curve and linear regression was applied. 
The squared coefficient of determination, R2 is greater than 0.97, indicating a reasonable fit to the data. Some 
scatter is seen in the measured data, which is related to a combination of the reading of the CO2 probe, the 
pressure probe and the mass flow controller. At steady state conditions, the CO2 probe had a tendency to 
fluctuate, therefore an average value over 5 min was taken as result. In theory, it is possible to obtain the overall 
mass transfer coefficient at each point by dividing the flux with the driving force as 𝐾𝑜𝑣 should be identical 
for all driving forces. This however can be very inaccurate due to the limitation of the CO2 probe and therefore 
the calibration curve is performed.  
Comparing the CA promoted MDEA solvents to that of unpromoted MDEA, it clearly reveals that the enzyme 
is enhancing the overall mass transfer of CO2 significantly. This is in line with the study by Penders-van Elk 
et al. [33] and Gladis et al. [72]. For 30 wt% MDEA with 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA, enhancements of a factor ≈ 3 
to 4 are seen. Interestingly, decreasing the MDEA concentration (0.15 wt% CA) has a positive effect on the 
overall mass transfer coefficient compared to 50 wt% MDEA with the same amount of enzyme.  
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 8 – Results and Discussion 
 
 
Page 46 of 134 
 
 
Figure 24: CO2 flux as a function of partial pressure of CO2 for unloaded aqueous MDEA (15 to 50 wt%) with CA (0 to 0.3 wt%) at 
298 K. Dots represent experimental data and lines represent linear regression. 
The overall mass transfer coefficient was additionally measured at different CO2 loading. 30 wt% MDEA with 
0.15 wt% CA at various loading and at 298, 313 and 328 K is presented in Figure 25. R2 values of ≥ 0.95 were 
obtained, which means that there is a linear dependency between the CO2 flux and the partial pressure. From 
the figure, it can be seen that the CO2 flux is decreasing with increasing loading, which corresponds to equation 
(30). In Figure 25.A absorption is observed for a CO2 loading up to 0.2 mol CO2/mol solvent. At 0.4 mol 
CO2/mol solvent desorption and absorption are seen. At 313 and 328 K desorption and absorption were seen 
at a CO2 loading of 0.1 mol CO2/mol solvent (Figure 25.B and C). Due to the accuracy of the CO2 probe, the 
first value in the measurement was not included. At 0.4 mol CO2/mol solvent, full desorption was observed at 
both temperatures.  
In a conventional CO2 capture process, using MDEA as a solvent, the rich solvent will leave the absorber at 
308 K with a loading of approximately 0.3 mol CO2/mol solvent [81]. As desorption is seen at 313 K using 
CA promoted MDEA it indicates that the absorption temperature should be lowered. Hence, the desorption 
temperature for the regeneration process; can as well be decreased to 328 K instead of 393 K.

8.3 Equilibrium Partial Pressure of CO2 
The equilibrium partial pressure of CO2 in the solvent is also a very important parameter in the design of an 
absorption and/or desorption process. The partial pressure of CO2 in the solvent can be obtained from Figure 
25 by interpolating to a CO2 flux of zero. The measured data were validated against the in-house model 
Extended UNIQUAC for MDEA at 298, 313 and 328 K, depicted in Figure 26. The dashed line in the figure 
indicates the deviation ranging from 0 to 25 %. The average prediction results in an average deviation of ± 12 
%. High deviations are seen at high equilibrium partial pressure of CO2 in the liquid.  
 
Figure 26: Predicted versus measured equilibrium partial pressure of CO2 in 15 to 50 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations from 0 to 
0.3 wt% at 298, 313 and 328 K. Dashed lines show the 25 % deviation ranges. The Extended UNIQUAC was used for the predicted 
values.  
Figure 27 shows measured partial pressures of CO2 in 15 to 50 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations from 0 to 
0.3 wt% CA. As depicted in Figure 27.A and B, the model predicts the equilibrium pressure in the CA 
promoted MDEA solvents relatively accurately and deviation from the measured data only occur at CO2 
loadings above 0.4 mol CO2/mol solvent. This indicates that the enzyme is not changing the solubility of 
MDEA. The systematic discrepancy occurring at high CO2 loading in the CA promoted solvents cannot be

a narrow opening creating an unstable liquid film and thereby giving an experimental error. An extra run at 
313 and 328 K and with various CO2 loading was made in order to improve the data. A good agreement with 
model and the measured data was however not obtained as shown in Figure 27.C. The two runs show although 
a good consistency.
