Taking into account the activity of the enzyme, the design of the absorption and desorption process will occur 
at much lower temperature than a conventional amine solvent. For the absorption process at 298 K, using CA 
promoted MDEA (30 wt%), the rich loaded solvent will leave the absorber with a loading above 0.5 mol 
CO2/mol solvent. In the desorption process occurring at 328 K the solvent will be stripped to a lean loading of 
approximately 0.2 mol CO2/mol solvent.

8.4 Liquid Side Mass Transfer Coefficient 
The liquid side mass transfer coefficient (𝑘𝑙
′) accounts for diffusion, physical solubility and chemical reaction 
of CO2 into the aqueous MDEA solvents as explained in section 5. This makes the liquid side mass transfer 
coefficient a very important parameter for comparison of solvents. A calculation example is found in appendix 
F.1. 
The liquid side mass transfer coefficient was determined for unloaded MDEA (30 wt%) without the enzyme. 
This in order to compare the effect of adding enzyme to the solvent. Experimental and predicted results of 𝑘𝑙
′ 
for 30 wt% MDEA are depicted in Figure 28. The model was calculated with equations (24) and (25). The 
experimental values are higher than the values obtained from the model and are decreasing with temperature 
whereas 𝑘𝑙
′ according to the model should increase with temperature. At 298 K, the kinetic rate constant was 
0.138 m3/(mol∙s), which is 10 % higher compared with data reported by Versteeg et al. [24]. This indicated 
that surface rippling caused the high values. Experiments with the addition of the surfactant Teepol to the 
solvent, which reduces rippling, carried out by Arne Gladis [72], showed results that were in accordance with 
the model. Furthermore, there was no difference in the absorption performance with and without the addition 
of Teepol in the CA promoted solvents and it was concluded that the enzyme solution received from 
Novozymes A/S had a similar effect as Teepol. In the absorption/desorption experiments including the enzyme 
it was decided to use the model to extract the kinetic rate constants of carbonic anhydrase. It should be 
mentioned that the effect of loading is not considered in the model. It was assumed that the contribution of 
MDEA to the CA enhanced absorption would be insignificant. 
 
Figure 28: Liquid side mass transfer coefficient of 30 wt% MDEA without enzyme compared to model as a function of temperature. 
Lines are calculated with equations (24) and (25).

Chapter 8 – Results and Discussion 
 
 
Page 52 of 134 
 
According to the model, the liquid side mass transfer coefficient of the aqueous MDEA solutions permits 
higher mass transfer at higher temperature. This is in accordance with most reaction kinetics, which are 
following an Arrhenius type of kinetics [82].  
The diffusion coefficient and the apparent Henry coefficient, but also physical properties such as density and 
viscosity are some of the temperature dependent parameters that are describing the liquid side mass transfer 
coefficient. Physical properties such as density and viscosity of the solvent are parameters, which can easily 
be measured. These properties were measured and used in the calculation of the gas side mass transfer 
coefficient (section 7.1). It was more difficult to obtain the diffusion- and the apparent Henry coefficients for 
CO2 loaded MDEA. Correlation of the diffusion coefficient was taken from the literature [68]. Commonly, the 
N2O analogy is used for determining the physical solubility. However, it has not been possible to find literature 
data on how 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 in MDEA is changing with CO2 loading. As the experimental data of the equilibrium 
pressure of carbon dioxide showed good agreement with the Extended UNIQUAC model, it seemed reasonable 
to extract the apparent Henry coefficient from the model (appendix G). Figure 29 gives a comparison of 
predicted 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 values obtained from the Extended UNIQUAC and the correlation proposed by Al-Ghawas et 
al. [68], which is based on the N2O analogy in unloaded MDEA solutions. 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 is increasing with increasing 
concentrations and temperatures using both approaches. The correlation proposed by Al-Ghawas et al. [68] is 
not valid at 328 K, which is illustrated in the figure. The solubility at 328 K gives similar values to 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 at 
313 K. Furthermore, 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 seems to have a decreasing trend with rising MDEA concentrations. The physical 
solubility is higher when using the thermodynamic model because the concentration of CO2 in the liquid is 
diluted compared to the bicarbonate and the carbonate concentrations. Whereas the N2O analogy does not 
account for solution species. Considering the experimental data should later be used in modelling of the 
absorption/desorption process it seems fair to use the Extended UNIQUAC. Using a thermodynamic model as 
the Extended UNIQUAC makes it possible to simulate the apparent Henrys coefficient at different CO2 
loading, MDEA concentrations and with all the ionic species produced in the liquid during reaction. This 
should give a more realistic estimate of 𝐻𝐶𝑂2, compared to the N2O analogy, which does not include the change 
in CO2 loading with respect to the MDEA solvent. It was assumed that the enzyme did not influence the 
thermodynamic properties in the MDEA solutions, as the experimental data of the equilibrium pressure 
compared with the model did not show any indication of this. A comparison of the catalytic activity of CA 
with the concentration of bicarbonate using the N2O analogy and the extended UNIQUAC to calculate 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 
will be presented in section 8.7.1.  
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 8 – Results and Discussion 
 
 
Page 53 of 134 
 
 
Figure 29: Henry coefficient versus MDEA concentration at 298, 313 and 328K. 
Addition of either 0.15 or 0.3 wt% CA to the aqueous MDEA solutions is significantly changing the liquid 
side mass transfer coefficient as shown in Figure 30. The addition of 0.15 wt% CA in respectively 15 and 30 
wt% MDEA (unloaded solutions) are accelerating the liquid mass transfer by a factor up to 9 at 298 K. The 
CO2 enhancement is although decreasing with increasing temperature and increasing MDEA concentration for 
unloaded solvents. The highest mass transfer occurs at low temperature and low MDEA concentration. This is 
the complete opposite to the liquid side mass transfer coefficient of MDEA without the enzyme. As previously 
described, two runs with 50 wt% MDEA with a CA concentration of 0.15 wt% were measured at 313 and 328 
K. The data point (marked run 2 in Figure 30) for the unloaded solvent at 313 K is off as it gives a higher mass 
transfer than expected. A small amount of the solvent ended in the condenser, which may have influenced the 
absorption. This point will further be presented as a blurred dot. A good agreement exists at 328 K between 
the two runs. The liquid side mass transfer coefficient is decreasing with CO2 loading and temperature for 30 
and 50 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA.

The influence of CO2 loading at 298, 313 and 328 K has an effect on the liquid side mass transfer coefficient. 
𝑘𝑙
′ decreases with loading, which is shown in Figure 31. The decreasing behaviour of 𝑘𝑙
′ with respect to loading 
can be caused by an increasing influence of the reverse and parallel reactions explained in sections 3 and 4. At 
high loading 𝑘𝑙
′ is relatively low, which means that the absorption also was very slow. In Figure 31.A and B, 
𝑘𝑙
′ is decreasing steadily and it seems to be caused by product inhibition of the enzyme. 𝑘𝑙
′ is increasing with 
decreasing MDEA concentrations, which can be seen by 15 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA having a much 
higher liquid mass transfer than e.g. 30 wt% MDEA with the same enzyme concentration. Even a higher 
concentration of CA in 30 wt% MDEA gives a lower 𝑘𝑙
′ at 298 and 313 K. For 50 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% 
CA, the liquid side mass transfer coefficient is much lower compared to 15 and 30 wt% MDEA.  
At 328 K (Figure 31.C) for MDEA (15 and 30 wt%) with respectively 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA, the liquid side 
mass transfer coefficients are more or less identical and are following the same trend. The liquid side mass 
transfer coefficient is decreasing very slowly. 𝑘𝑙
′ is decreasing in 30 wt% MDEA with a CA concentration of 
0.15 wt%. For 50 wt% MDEA, 𝑘𝑙
′ is starting at a lower value and then it rises and approaching the same values 
as 30 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA at higher loading.

A typical absorption temperature is at 313 K for aqueous amine solvents. Addition of enzyme to the solvent 
would mean a lowering of the absorption temperature to 298 K or even less in order to get the advantage on 
the mass transfer, which was already seen by measuring 𝐾𝑜𝑣. Higher temperatures will also lead to a 
deactivation of CA.  
Comparing the liquid side mass transfer coefficients of CA promoted MDEA (15 to 50 wt%) solutions with 
the benchmark 30 wt% MEA listed in Table 8, it reveals that the addition of carbonic anhydrase does not 
enhance the CO2 absorption in a way that it can compete with MEA. The mass transfer of 30 wt% MEA is 
respectively 3.5 and 5 times higher compared to 15 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA and 30 wt% MDEA with 
0.15 wt% CA. It could be interesting to reduce the MDEA concentration or increase the CA concentration to 
0.3 wt% in 15 wt% MDEA as the absorption rate seems to rise with decreasing MDEA concentration and 
rising enzyme concentration.

8.5 Enzyme Reaction Rate Constants for CA 
The addition of carbonic anhydrase is accelerating the reaction kinetics by lowering the activation energy of 
the MDEA solutions. In order to determine if the reaction kinetics can be considered pseudo first order, the 
Hatta number and the infinite enhancement factor are calculated using equations (32) and (36). The values are 
listed in Table 9. According to equation (35), the Hatta number should be above two and much smaller than 
the infinite enhancement factor in order to fulfil the pseudo first order assumption. This is fulfilled for the 
experiments as shown in the table.  
Table 9: Calculated values of Ha and E∞ for CA promoted MDEA solutions at the temperature range 298 to 328 K and the loading 
range 0 to 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent. Equations (32) and (36) have been used.

8.5.1 Overall Enzyme Reaction Rate Constant (1/s) of CA 
The overall enzyme reaction rate constant, 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 (1/s) was calculated by subtracting equations (16) and (7). 
A calculation example and tables containing the reaction rate constants of CA are available in appendix H. 
The overall enzyme reaction rate constant as a function of temperature for MDEA concentrations of 15 to 50 
wt% with the addition of 0.15 wt% CA is presented in Figure 32. The catalytic activity of CA in 15 wt% 
MDEA (unloaded) is decreasing with increasing temperature. The same tendency is not seen for 30 wt% 
MDEA (unloaded), here the enzyme reaction rate constant is more or less unaffected by temperature and only 
a minor decrease is seen in Figure 32. At 298 and 313 K, 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 is unaffected of the unloaded solvent 
concentrations giving values in the same magnitude, which corresponds to the observation by Penders-van Elk 
et al. [33]. The MDEA concentration seems to have a greater effect on the overall enzymatic rate constant at 
328 K. The decrease in enzyme activity at 328 K may be caused by a deactivation of the enzyme. The overall 
catalytic activity of CA in 50 wt% MDEA does not match the expected. It was assumed that 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 would 
follow a similar trend as in 15 and 30 wt% MDEA. From these results, the overall enzyme activity is increasing 
with temperature and gives a much higher value at 328 K compared to 15 and 30 wt% MDEA. There seems 
to be a systematic error in the measurements as earlier described. 
Loading the solutions with CO2 is decreasing the catalytic activity of the enzyme by a factor ≈ 2 at 298 K. 
𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 seems to increase from 298 K to 313 K and decrease at 328 K in 15 and 30 wt% MDEA. The reverse 
reaction can have an influence on the rate constant due to the CO2 loading and desorption were seen in 30 wt% 
MDEA. The complete opposite is seen in 50 wt% MDEA, where the overall enzyme reaction rate is increasing.

An increasing linear tendency between the overall enzyme reaction rate and the CA concentration (0 to 0.3 
wt%), indicating a first order reaction rate with respect to CA, is observed in Figure 33. This is in line with 
results presented by Penders-van Elk et al. [33] [55] and Gladis et al. [72]. Interestingly, the influence of 
temperature on the enzyme activity with the addition of 0.15 wt% CA in 30 wt% MDEA seems to be 
independent at 298 and 313 K. This independency of the enzyme activity is overcome by rising the CA 
concentration. 
At 298 K, 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 in the CO2 loaded solvent is increasing linearly. The activity is strongly dependent of the 
CO2 concentration, CA concentration and the temperature. Hence, the enzyme activity at 298 K is lowered 
significantly compared to the unloaded solvent. At 328 K, the catalytic activity of CA seems to be increasing 
non-linear and with a CA concentration of 0.3 wt% the overall reaction rate constant is higher than for the 
unloaded solvent. At 328 K, desorption and absorption occurred for the CO2 loaded MDEA solvent. It seemed 
faster to strip of the CO2 than to absorb it in the solvent. As desorption and absorption are occurring in the 
solvent at the same time it also complicates the interpretation of the reaction rate because the forward and the 
reverse reactions then become important.

Dots: Measured value, Lines: Regression. 
The influence on the concentration of MDEA to the overall enzyme reaction rate was also studied. The results 
are shown in Figure 34. At 298 K, the MDEA concentration has almost no influence on 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 and only an

insignificant decrease is observed in 30 wt% MDEA. It seems that CA at this specific temperature does not

anhydrase is enhancing the CO2 reaction with water (reaction (iii) in section 3) at 298 K [33]. 
Increasing the temperature is increasing the influence of the MDEA concentration on 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧. The overall 
catalytic activity of CA has a negative effect when increasing the solvent concentration to 30 wt%. A positive 
influence on 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 in 50 wt% MDEA at 313 and 328 K is seen. However, these data points are not considered 
to be very accurate due to the limitation of the WWC. The negative effect on 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 at 313 and 328 K in 30 
wt% MDEA indicates that CA has an influence on the CO2 reactions with water and MDEA. This also 
corresponds to the intermolecular proton transfer being the rate limiting part in the regeneration of the enzyme 
activity as the catalytic activity is decreasing with increasing MDEA concentration.

8.5.2 Enzyme Reaction Rate Constant (m3/(mol·s)) 
The second order enzyme reaction rate constant 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 (m3/(mol·s)) was derived from the overall reaction rate 
constant 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧 divided by the enzyme concentration.  
The temperature dependency on 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 for CA (0.15 and 0.3 wt%) promoted MDEA solvents (15 to 50 wt%) is 
pictured in Figure 35. The enzyme reaction rate constant is decreasing with increasing temperatures and 
MDEA concentrations. The trend of the enzyme activity in MDEA concentrations from 15 to 30 wt% is a 
minor increase to 313 K and a decrease at 328 K. This observation is in line with the previously observations

on the effect of the temperature on the liquid side mass transfer coefficient, where a lower mass transfer 
occurred at high temperatures. 30 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 wt% are less affected 
by the temperature and are having the same value of the enzyme activity. The catalytic activity of CA in 15 
wt% MDEA at 328 K is approaching the same values as in 30 wt% MDEA. This tendency was also seen for 
the liquid side mass transfer coefficient in Figure 30.  The influence of MDEA on the catalytic activity of CA 
is negligible at 298 K. The decreasing behaviour at 328 K is due to the enzyme starts to deactivate. Rising the 
temperature above 328 K will need a thermostable carbonic anhydrase strain. The systematic discrepancy seen 
in 50 wt% MDEA, is given unreliable results.  
 
Figure 35: Influence of temperature on the CA reaction rate constant for unloaded MDEA (15 to 50 wt%) with 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA. 
Blurred dots: Systematic error in the measurements. 
Based on the behaviour that CO2 loading had on the liquid side mass transfer coefficient, it was expected to 
see a decrease in the enzyme activity for the loaded MDEA solvents. As depicted in Figure 36, the enzyme 
activity in the MDEA solvents is clearly decreasing with increasing CO2 loading. It seems reasonable that the 
decrease is due to inhibition. The chemical equilibrium favours CO2, as the bicarbonate concentration in the 
solvents is rising, therefore the reverse and forward reactions are occurring. The catalytic activity of CA is 
highest in low MDEA concentration at 298 and 313 K. 
The addition of either 0.15 or 0.3 wt% CA in 30 wt% MDEA does not have an influence on the enzyme 
reaction rate constant with respect to temperature and CO2 loading as shown in Figure 36, which is a very 
interesting observation as the amount of enzyme could be reduced. At 328 K (Figure 36.C), 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 is lowered, 
but interestingly the loading appears to have a minor effect on the enzyme activity as it seems to have reached 
an almost constant level. The values of the enzyme activity in 15 wt% MDEA are similar to 30 wt% MDEA

The enzyme reaction rate constant versus the CA concentration in unloaded and CO2 loaded solvents is 
presented in Figure 37. The catalytic activity of the enzyme is linear dependent up to a CA concentration of 
0.15 wt% and deviates at higher enzyme concentrations. The enzyme concentration has less influence on the 
catalytic activity when CO2 is added to the solvents. This can be seen by a significant decrease in the 
acceleration compared to 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 in unloaded MDEA as depicted in the figure. The enzyme activity in 30 wt% 
MDEA seems to reach a maximum in the concentration range 0.15 to 0.3 wt% CA, which is in line with 
Michaelis-Menten kinetics. This applies to the unloaded and CO2 loaded solvent. In order to verify the 
assumption more experiments need to be conducted with enzyme concentrations from 0 to 0.25 wt%. The 
catalytic activity of CA is slightly decreasing at an enzyme concentration of 0.3 wt%, which can be related to 
an experimental error.

The influence of MDEA on 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 revealed to be the same as already discussed in the previous section 7.4.1 and

𝑘𝐻2𝑂
∗
= 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑤𝑖𝑡ℎ 𝐶𝐴−𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑤𝑖𝑡ℎ𝑜𝑢𝑡 𝐶𝐴

(81) 
with 𝑘𝐻2𝑂
∗
 being the corrected enzymatic rate constant in m3/(mol∙s). The corrected enzymatic rate constant as 
a function of the CA concentration in MDEA (15 and 30 wt%) is presented in Figure 38. The figure shows a 
linear dependency meaning that the enzyme catalysed CO2 absorption is first order in water. The results 
indicate that the MDEA concentration with the addition of 0.15 wt% CA does not contribute significantly to 
the enhancement at 298 and 313 K, which in line with Penders-van Elk et al. [32][38][54]. The enzymatic rate 
constant starts to deviate at 328 K comparing with the 15 and 30 wt% MDEA solutions. The rate constant is 
decreasing with decreasing MDEA concentration. This is in line with the observation on the influence of 
MDEA on the overall enzymatic rate constant in Figure 34.

8.6 Inhibition Model 
 
The decrease in the catalytic activity of the enzyme when CO2 loading was applied, can be described by the 
inhibition of the produced bicarbonate (section 4.2.2): 
 
𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧= 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧𝐶𝐶𝐴
1 + 𝐶𝐻𝐶𝑂3−
𝐾𝑖
 
(82) 
with 𝐾𝑖 being the inhibition constant expressed in mol/m3 and 𝐶𝐻𝐶𝑂3− being the bicarbonate concentration 
(mol/m3) obtained from the Extended UNIQUAC model:  
 
𝐶𝐻𝐶𝑂3−=
𝑥𝐻𝐶𝑂3
−𝜌𝑠𝑜𝑙
∑𝑥𝑖𝑀𝑖
𝑖
 
(83) 
where 𝑥𝐻𝐶𝑂3− is the mole fraction of HCO3- and 𝜌𝑠𝑜𝑙 is the density of the solution expressed in kg/m3. The 
density is obtained from the correlation proposed by Weiland et al. [78]. 𝑥 and 𝑀 are respectively the mole 
fraction and the molar mass. 𝑖 represents the species: H2O, CO2, MDEA, H+, MDEAH+, OH-, CO32- and HCO3-
. Ki and 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 are fitted using the least squares approach by minimizing the objective function: 
 
𝑆= ∑(𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧,𝑖
𝑚𝑒𝑎𝑠𝑢𝑟𝑒𝑑−𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧,𝑖
𝑝𝑟𝑒𝑑𝑖𝑐𝑡𝑒𝑑)
2
𝑖
 
(84) 
𝑖 represents the corresponding measured and predicted values. The Excel solver add-in is used to perform the 
minimization of the objective function. The minimization of the objective function results in an average 
deviation of ± 14 % and a maximum deviation of 70 %. The greatest deviation is found in 50 wt% MDEA. 
The fitted parameters are listed in Table 10. The inhibition constant is not influenced by the concentration of 
respectively MDEA or CA; it is however influenced by temperature. The inhibition constant is rising with 
increasing temperatures. 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 in 30  wt% MDEA is not influenced by the temperature. 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 is changing with 
respect to temperature and MDEA concentration. This may indicate that the solvent has an inhibiting effect on 
the enzyme.  
Table 10: Fitted kenz and Ki for CA concentrations of 0.15 to 0.3 wt% in 15 to 50 wt% MDEA at 298, 313 and 328 K. Equations (82)and 
(84) have been used to fit the parameters.

The inhibition constant is plotted versus temperature as shown in Figure 39. 𝐾𝑖 is following a Arrhenius type 
of equation, which means that the inhibition constant is increasing exponentially in the temperature range 298 
to 328 K. The affinity of bicarbonate binding is decreasing with increasing 𝐾𝑖 corresponding to the observation 
in Figure 36 where 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 at 328 K was almost not affected by CO2 loading.  
==PAGE:88==

Figure 39: Ki versus temperature. Dots: Ki, Line: Exponentially regression.   
The influence of temperature on the inhibition constant is expressed by: 
 
𝐾𝑖= 3.48 ⋅10−4 ⋅exp (4.53 ⋅10−2 ⋅𝑇) 
(85) 
with 𝑇 being the temperature in kelvin. Equation (85) is only valid in the range 298 to 328 K. The inhibition 
model, equation (82), is validated by plotting the predicted values versus the measured data to see if it results 
in a straight line. The model is validated at 298, 313 and 328 K and with MDEA concentrations from 15 to 50 
wt%. The deviation range indicate that the average deviation is within ± 14 %. The plot is depicted in Figure 
40. The data points are following a straight line relatively accurate. There is a good agreement with the 
predicted values and the measured values in 15 wt% MDEA. The model prediction seems to slightly 
underestimate the catalytic activity of CA with an enzyme concentration of 0.15 wt% and slightly overestimate 
it with a CA concentration of 0.3 wt% in 30 wt% MDEA at 298 and 313 K. The reverse tendency is observed 
at 328 K. The model prediction of 50 wt% MDEA is significantly underpredicting the measured values. It is 
also here the greatest deviation is found between the measured and the predicted values. Based on the 
experimental results in 50 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA there were quite a lot of scatter in the measurements 
due to some difficulties of keeping a steady liquid film.

The model and the measured values are furthermore validated by plotting the catalytic activity of CA versus 
the bicarbonate concentration at 298, 313 and 328 K. A decreasing trend of the enzyme activity at 298, 313 
and 328 K is seen with rising bicarbonate concentration shown in Figure 41. The proposed model is relatively 
accurate in predicting the inhibition of bicarbonate in 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 at 298 K compared to the measured data. Outliers 
are seen for 50 wt% MDEA. At 313 K (Figure 41.B), the deviation is increasing with rising bicarbonate and 
MDEA concentrations. Figure 41.B clearly outlines that there is a certain amount of experimental scatter in 50 
wt% MDEA. The model prediction is very accurate for 15 wt% MDEA and no systematic error is seen. 
Increasing the temperature to 328 K (Figure 41.C) is increasing the deviation when comparing the model and 
the measured values. The model is underpredicting 30 wt% MDEA with 0.3 wt% CA and over predicting 30 
wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA. Increasing the temperature is also increasing the bicarbonate concentration 
in the solvents, which may explain the increasing deviation with increasing temperatures. Since, inhibition is 
clearly coursing the decrease in the catalytic activity of CA when CO2 loading is applied the reverse reaction 
should be implemented when determining 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧.

Overall, the model seems to predict the inhibition of bicarbonate relatively accurate and is in good agreement 
with the measured 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 in 15 and 30 wt% MDEA. The average prediction is ± 14 % in the temperature range 
298 to 328 K. The model is not valid for 50 wt% MDEA.  
8.6.1 Evaluation of fitted parameters 
Equation (82) was also fitted to results where the N2O analogy was used to calculate the apparent Henry 
coefficient. The purpose was to see how big an influence the two approaches of estimating 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 have on 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧. 
The fitted parameters based on the N2O analogy and the Extended UNIQIUAC are listed in Table 11. There is 
a clear difference in the two approaches, which means that the Henry coefficient plays a significant role when 
determining the enzyme kinetics. The inhibition constant derived on basis of the N2O analogy is increasing 
with temperature and solvent concentration. The inhibition constant derived on basis of the Extended 
UNIQUAC is only temperature dependent. 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 is decreasing with increasing temperature and increasing  
MDEA concentration when using both approaches. It appears to be more accurate to use the Extended 
UNIQUAC as the Henry coefficient is dependent on CO2 loading. The N2O analogy for MDEA found in the 
literature does not account for this. Additionally, it seems more reasonable that the inhibition constant is a 
function of the temperature. These considerations suggest that 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 estimated from the Extended UNIQUAC 
is more reliable.  
Table 11: Evaluation between fitted kenz and Ki in 15 and 30 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA at 298, 313 
and 328 K using the Henry coefficient calculated from the N2O analogy and the Extended UNIQUAC.

Extended UNIQUAC

8.6.2 Liquid mass transfer coefficient 
Based on equations (82) and (24) it was possible to calculate the liquid side mass transfer coefficient in 15 and 
30 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 wt% at 298 to 328 K. The calculated 𝑘𝑙
′ was validated 
against the measured values and are depicted in Figure 42. The dashed line in the figure is showing the 
deviation range up to 15 %. 50 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA was not included, since the model, equations 
(82), is not valid for the high concentrated MDEA solvent. The model is predicting the measured values within 
an average of ± 15 %, which is considered relatively accurate. In particular, the prediction of the liquid side 
mass transfer coefficient in 15 wt% MDEA is in good agreement with the measured values giving an average 
prediction of ± 5 %. The model has in general a tendency to overestimate the measured values.

decreasing with increasing temperatures and rising MDEA concentrations, which is in line with the 
measurements. There is a good agreement between the model and the measured values and only one significant

tendency to overpredict the measured values, which is shown in the figure. There is overall a good agreement 
between the calculated and the experimental values and equation (82) is therefore assumed accurate. Equation 
(82) allows interpolation between the measured ranges of 15 to 30 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations of

MDEA respectively.

8.7 Mixture of MDEA and K2CO3 
CA enhanced CO2 absorption was studied in a mixture of MDEA and K2CO3. It was assumed that by mixing 
MDEA and K2CO3 the benefit of a high mass transfer at low MDEA concentrations and high mass transfer at 
high K2CO3 concentrations would result in a better CO2 absorption performance. Solubility tests of mixing the 
two compounds, listed in Table 13, showed that a concentration of 10 wt% K2CO3 in 30 wt% MDEA was the 
solubility limit. Above this concentration, two liquid phases are created, depicted in Figure 44.

The overall mass transfer coefficient was measured for the unloaded solution of 30 wt% MDEA + 10 wt% 
K2CO3 with a CA concentration of 0.15 wt%. 𝐾𝑜𝑣 was extracted from the plot of the CO2 flux as a function of 
the mean logarithmic pressure of CO2 as shown in Figure 45. Comparing the result of the overall mass transfer 
coefficient with the one of 30 wt% MDEA with 0.15 wt% CA it shows that the CO2 absorption rate is decreased 
significantly. An enhancement has though occurred compared to 30 wt% MDEA without the enzyme.

Chapter 9 – Conclusion and Future Work 
 
 
Page 73 of 134 
 
9   Conclusion and Future Work  
The kinetics of carbonic anhydrase enhanced CO2 absorption into MDEA was studied on a wetted wall column. 
MDEA concentrations from 15 to 50 wt% with CA concentrations of 0.15 and 0.3 % were studied at 298, 313 
and 328 K and with a CO2 loading range from 0 to 0.5 mol CO2/mol solvent. 
Physical properties of MDEA with CA showed that density and viscosity respectively decreased and increased 
compared to MDEA without enzyme. Density and viscosity correlations were made in order to reduce the 
experimental work. The average prediction of the density was 0.5 % and the average prediction of the viscosity 
was 3 %.  
Determination of the equilibrium partial pressure of CO2 in the CA promoted MDEA solvents revealed that 
decreasing the temperature will result in a higher rich loading. The CA promoted solvents were further 
compared to the Extended UNIQUAC. Results showed that the enzyme did not change the thermodynamic 
properties in MDEA and there was a good agreement between the model prediction and the measured data in 
15 and 30 wt% MDEA with addition of enzyme. The model was used to calculate the apparent Henry 
coefficient.  
Enhancement of a factor 4 was seen in 30 wt% MDEA with a CA concentration of 0.3 wt% compared to 30 
wt% MDEA without the enzyme. The liquid side mass transfer coefficient of the CA promoted solvents 
decreased with increasing temperature and increasing MDEA concentration, indicating that the benefit of the 
mass transfer occur at low temperature and solvent concentration. Loading the solvents with CO2 decreased 
the liquid side mass transfer significantly.  
The kinetic rate constants of CA showed that the enzyme is less active with increasing temperatures and 
increasing MDEA concentrations, which indicates that the solvent has an inhibiting effect at high 
concentrations. Results revealed that CA is enhancing the CO2 reaction with water at 298 K. At 313 and 328 
K both CO2 reactions with water and MDEA reactions are enhanced by the enzyme. The catalytic activity of 
CA decreased significantly as CO2 loading was applied, which indicated inhibition of bicarbonate. A simple 
model was developed based on the modified Michaelis-Menten kinetic to describe the decrease in enzyme 
activity. The model predicted the measured values within an average of ± 14 %. The derived model allows  
The CA concentration has a minor influence on the reaction rate constant when CO2 is applied, as results 
showed values in the same magnitude for 30 wt% MDEA with respectively 0.15 and 0.3 wt% CA.  
 For a CA enhanced MDEA CO2 capture process the temperature of the absorption process should be lowered 
in order to get the benefit of a high mass transfer. The best CA enhanced CO2 absorption rate was seen in 15 
wt% MDEA as it was 34 % faster than 30 wt% MDEA and 60 % faster than 50 wt% MDEA with the same 
amount of enzyme. 
==PAGE:97==
Chapter 9 – Conclusion and Future Work 
 
 
Page 74 of 134 
 
9.1 Future work 
Relevant areas that could be interesting to study further are: 
Wetted wall column experiments: 
 
Absorption experiments in MDEA with the addition of the surfactant Teepol. As surface rippling 
resulted in higher kinetic rates constants compared to literature.  
 
Absorption study of the mixture 15 wt% MDEA + 20 wt% K2CO3 with 0.15 wt% CA. Experiments 
carried out by Ph.D. student Arne Gladis [72], showed that the highest absorption rates were seen with 
20 wt% K2CO3 and in this study the best mass transfer was achieved with 15 wt% MDEA.  
 
Addition of metal ions into the CA enhanced MDEA solvents could be interesting to investigate as 
literature [58] suggest that this could increase the enzyme activity. 
Pilot scale: 
 
An interesting area is to upscale the absorption/desorption experiments in order to study the 
performance of the enzyme activity on an absorber unit and compare these results with the ones 
obtained in this thesis. A test like this would give some valuable information on the stability of the 
enzyme under more realistic CO2 capture conditions. A pilot scale absorber is available at Center for 
Energy Resources Engineering (CERE), DTU.  
Simulation: 
 
Simulation of the CO2 absorption performance in CA enhanced MDEA solvents using Aspen Plus and 
validate it against experimental data obtained from the wetted wall column and/or pilot plant.

D.5 Viscosity correlation 
A correlation of the viscosity at different concentrations of CA was made from the measured values listed in  
Table 25. Viscosities were plotted versus the weight percent of carbonic anhydrase and from the best fit of the 
data, the following equations were obtained: 
 
𝜂𝐶𝐴,298𝐾= 0.1204 ⋅(𝑤𝑡%)2 −0.0927 ⋅𝑤𝑡% + 0.928     
(90) 
 
𝜂𝐶𝐴,313𝐾= 0.0997 ⋅(𝑤𝑡%)2 −0.0975 ⋅𝑤𝑡% + 0.7479         
(91) 
 
𝜂𝐶𝐴,328𝐾= 0.1516 ⋅(𝑤𝑡%)2 −0.1808 ⋅𝑤𝑡% + 0.5369     
(92)

In order to make the viscosity correlation of CA temperature dependent, equations (90) to (92) were plotted as 
a function of the temperature in kelvin as shown in Figure 49. The figure depicts that there is a linear correlation 
between viscosity and temperature for 0.15 and 0.3 wt% of carbonic anhydrase.

E – CO2 flux and mean logarithmic partial pressure of CO2  
In this appendix the graphical plots of the CO2 flux versus the mean logarithmic pressure of CO2 are presented 
for all solvents. A calculation example of the CO2 flux and the mean logarithmic pressure in unloaded 30 wt% 
MDEA at 298 K is presented in this appendix. The used equations are from section 7.3.1. Data for 30 wt% 
MDEA at 298 K is presented in tables. Due to the huge amount of data, it has been decided not show all raw 
data for all solvents at the different temperatures. 
E.1 Calculation of the CO2 flux and the mean logarithmic partial pressure of CO2 
The following calculation example is based on 30 wt% MDEA at 298 K and without CO2 loading. The mole 
flux of inert gas is calculated using equation (62):

F – Mass transfer  
The mass transfer parameters in 15 to 50 wt% MDEA with CA concentrations from 0 to 0.3 wt% are presented. 
Additionally, is a calculation example of the liquid side mass transfer coefficient in 30 wt% MDEA at 298 K 
without CO2 loading shown. 
F.1 Calculation example of the liquid side mass transfer coefficient in 30 wt% MDEA at 298 K 
The liquid side mass transfer coefficient is obtained from equation (31). The overall mass transfer coefficient 
is determined experimentally and the value was 1.36·10-7 mol/(Pa·m2·s) (Table 33). The gas side mass transfer 
coefficient is obtained from the Sherwood’s number analogy, equation (42). The hydraulic diameter is obtained 
from the outer diameter minus the diameter of the column containing the liquid film.

G – Apparent Henry coefficient for CO2 in MDEA 
This appendix presents the apparent Henry coefficient obtained from the Extended UNIQUAC and a 
calculation example. 
The apparent Henry coefficient (Pa·m3/mol) was derived from the Extended UNIQUAC model. In order to use 
the model, it was assumed that the addition of CA had no influence on the solubility of the aqueous MDEA 
solutions. Moles of MDEA, CO2 and water are given as input variables in the model. The input of CO2 is given 
by: 
 
𝑛𝐶𝑂2 = 𝑛𝑀𝐷𝐸𝐴⋅𝛼 
(93) 
with 𝛼 being the CO2 loading in mol CO2/mol solvent. The model gives the output variables of the different 
species in the liquid, bubble point pressure and partial pressure. The moles of each species in the liquid and 
the partial pressure of CO2 are extracted and the concentration of CO2 (mol/m3) is obtained by: 
 
𝐶𝐶𝑂2  = 𝑥𝐶𝑂2 ⋅𝜌𝑠𝑜𝑙,𝛼
∑𝑥𝑖𝑀𝑖
𝑖
 
(94) 
where 𝜌𝑠𝑜𝑙,𝛼 is the density of the solution at different CO2 loadings expressed in kg/m3. The density is obtained 
from the correlation proposed by Weiland et al. [78] (appendix D). 𝑥 and 𝑀 are respectively the mole fraction 
and the molar mass where 𝑖 represents the species: H2O, CO2, MDEA, H+, MDEAH+, OH-, CO32- and HCO3-. 
The Henry coefficient of carbon dioxide in the liquid at different loadings is defined as: 
 
𝐻𝐶𝑂2  = 𝑝𝐶𝑂2
𝐶𝐶𝑂2
 
(95) 
The partial pressure of carbon dioxide is obtained from the output of the model. The simulated apparent Henry 
coefficient versus CO2 loading in MDEA (15 to 50 wt%) at 298, 313 and 328 K is depicted in Figure 55. 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 
in 15 wt% MDEA (Figure 55.A) is decreasing with loading at 298 K and increasing at 313 and 328 K. The 
physical solubility in 30 and 50 wt% MDEA at 298 K (Figure 55.B and C) is decreasing with increasing CO2 
loading. The trend at 313 and 328 K is an increase of the apparent Henry coefficient at low CO2 concentrations 
and then it is decreasing with increasing CO2 loading. In general 𝐻𝐶𝑂2 is increasing with temperature and rising 
MDEA concentration.

H – Enzymatic rate constants 
Tables with the derived overall rate constant of MDEA with an without CA (1/s) and the enzymatic rate 
constant (m3/(mol·s)) are presented in this appendix. A calculation example of 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 in 15 wt% MDEA at 298 
K and with a CO2 loading of 0.24 mol CO2/mol solvent is shown.

H.2 Calculation example 
 Calculation example of 𝑘𝑒𝑛𝑧 in 15 wt% MDEA at 298 K and with a CO2 loading of 0.24 mol CO2/mol solvent 
is shown. The overall reaction rate constant of 15 wt% MDEA with CA is obtained from equation (37). The 
Henry coefficient is 3712 (Pa·m3)/mol and the diffusion coefficient is 1.68·10-9 m2/s.

wt% MDEA is shown in Table 40. The overall reaction rate constant of CA is obtained by subtracting  𝑘𝑜𝑣 
with enzyme with 𝑘𝑜𝑣 without the enzyme. 
𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑒𝑛𝑧= 𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑤𝑖𝑡ℎ 𝑒𝑛𝑧𝑦𝑚𝑒−𝑘𝑜𝑣,𝑤𝑖𝑡ℎ𝑜𝑢𝑡 𝑒𝑛𝑧𝑦𝑚𝑒= 1.95 ⋅103 1
